srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/%D8%B1%D8%B3%D9%85_%D8%AA%D8%B9%D8%A8%D9%8A%D8%B1%D9%8A_%D9%84%D9%84%D9%81%D8%B8_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%AC%D9%84%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%A9_%D9%88%D9%85%D9%86_%D9%8A%D8%AC%D9%84%D9%87%D9%85_%D8%A3%D9%87%D9%84_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%B3%D9%86%D8%A9.png/330px-%D8%B1%D8%B3%D9%85_%D8%AA%D8%B9%D8%A8%D9%8A%D8%B1%D9%8A_%D9%84%D9%84%D9%81%D8%B8_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%AC%D9%84%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%A9_%D9%88%D9%85%D9%86_%D9%8A%D8%AC%D9%84%D9%87%D9%85_%D8%A3%D9%87%D9%84_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%B3%D9%86%D8%A9.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/%D8%B1%D8%B3%D9%85_%D8%AA%D8%B9%D8%A8%D9%8A%D8%B1%D9%8A_%D9%84%D9%84%D9%81%D8%B8_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%AC%D9%84%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%A9_%D9%88%D9%85%D9%86_%D9%8A%D8%AC%D9%84%D9%87%D9%85_%D8%A3%D9%87%D9%84_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%B3%D9%86%D8%A9.png/440px-%D8%B1%D8%B3%D9%85_%D8%AA%D8%B9%D8%A8%D9%8A%D8%B1%D9%8A_%D9%84%D9%84%D9%81%D8%B8_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%AC%D9%84%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%A9_%D9%88%D9%85%D9%86_%D9%8A%D8%AC%D9%84%D9%87%D9%85_%D8%A3%D9%87%D9%84_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%B3%D9%86%D8%A9.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D8%B1%D8%B3%D9%85_%D8%AA%D8%B9%D8%A8%D9%8A%D8%B1%D9%8A_%D9%84%D9%84%D9%81%D8%B8_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%AC%D9%84%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%A9_%D9%88%D9%85%D9%86_%D9%8A%D8%AC%D9%84%D9%87%D9%85_%D8%A3%D9%87%D9%84_%D8%A7%D9%84%D8%B3%D9%86%D8%A9.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The calligraphic representation of religious Sunni Islamic figures, such as <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Umar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uthman" title="Uthman">Uthman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_Ali" title="Hasan ibn Ali">Hasan ibn Ali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Husayn_ibn_Ali" title="Husayn ibn Ali">Husayn ibn Ali</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a> (God).</div></div></div>
<p><b>Sunni Islam</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/uË/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uË</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/Ê/: 'u' in 'push'">Ê</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>) is the largest <a href="/wiki/Islamic_schools_and_branches" title="Islamic schools and branches">branch</a> of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, followed by 85â90% of the world's <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a>. Its name comes from the word <i><a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">Sunnah</a></i>, referring to the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The differences between Sunni and <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia Muslims</a> arose from a disagreement over the <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_Muhammad" title="Succession to Muhammad">succession to Muhammad</a> and subsequently acquired broader political significance, as well as <a href="/wiki/Schools_of_Islamic_theology" title="Schools of Islamic theology">theological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">juridical</a> dimensions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMMENA_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-EMMENA-2">[2]</a></sup> According to Sunni traditions, Muhammad left no successor and the participants of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saqifah" title="Saqifah">Saqifah event</a> appointed <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a> as the next-in-line (the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">caliph</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EMMENA_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-EMMENA-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FitzpatrickWalkerP3_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FitzpatrickWalkerP3-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> This contrasts with the <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam#Succession_of_Ali" title="Shia Islam">Shia view</a>, which holds that Muhammad appointed his son-in-law and cousin <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali ibn Abi Talib</a> as his successor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The adherents of Sunni Islam are referred to in <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> as <span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">ahl as-sunnah wa l-jamÄÊ»ah</i></span> ("the people of the Sunnah and the community") or <span title="American Library Association â Library of Congress transliteration"><i lang="ar-Latn">ahl as-Sunnah</i></span> for short. In English, its doctrines and practices are sometimes called <i>Sunnism</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> while adherents are known as Sunni Muslims, Sunnis, Sunnites and Ahlus Sunnah. Sunni Islam is sometimes referred to as "orthodox Islam",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> though some scholars view this translation as inappropriate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, together with <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a> (especially those collected in <a href="/wiki/Kutub_al-Sittah" title="Kutub al-Sittah">Kutub al-Sittah</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Ijma" title="Ijma">binding juristic consensus</a>, form the basis of all <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">traditional jurisprudence</a> within Sunni Islam. <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a> rulings are derived from these basic sources, in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/Qiyas" title="Qiyas">analogical reasoning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Istislah" title="Istislah">consideration</a> of <a href="/wiki/Maslaha" title="Maslaha">public welfare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Istihsan" title="Istihsan">juristic discretion</a>, using the <a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Islamic_jurisprudence" title="Principles of Islamic jurisprudence">principles of jurisprudence</a> developed by the traditional <a href="/wiki/Madhhab" title="Madhhab">legal schools</a>. In matters of <a href="/wiki/Aqidah" title="Aqidah">creed</a>, the Sunni tradition upholds the six pillars of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iman_(concept)" title="Iman (concept)">imÄn</a></i> (faith) and comprises the <a href="/wiki/Ash%27ari" title="Ash'ari">Ash'ari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maturidi" title="Maturidi">Maturidi</a> schools of <a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">Kalam</a> (theology) as well as the textualist school known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditionalist_Theology_(Islam)" title="Traditionalist Theology (Islam)">traditionalist theology</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sunna">Sunna</span></h3>
<p>The Arabic term <i>Sunna</i>, according to which Sunnis are named, is old and roots in pre-Islamic language. It was used for traditions which a majority of people followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The term got greater political significance after the murder of the third <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni_Caliph" title="Sunni Caliph">Sunni Caliph</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CA%BFUthm%C4%81n_ibn_%CA%BFAff%C4%81n" title="Ê¿UthmÄn ibn Ê¿AffÄn">Ê¿UthmÄn ibn Ê¿AffÄn</a>. It is said MÄlik al-Aschtar, a famous follower of <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ê¿AlÄ« ibn AbÄ« TÄlib</a>, encouraged during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Siffin" title="Battle of Siffin">battle of Siffin</a> with the expression, Ali's enemy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muawiyah_I" title="Muawiyah I">MuÊ¿Äwiya ibn AbÄ« SufyÄn</a> kills the <i>Sunna</i>. After the battle, it was agreed that "the righteous <i><a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">Sunnah</a></i>, the unifying, not the divisive" ("<i>as-sunna al-Ê¿Ädila al-Ç§ÄmiÊ¿a Ä¡air al-mufarriqa</i>") should be consulted to resolve the conflict. The time when the term <i>sunna</i> became the short form for "<a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">prophet</a>" (<i>sunnat an-nabÄ«</i>) is still unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a>, several political movements, amongst them the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shia" title="Shia">Shia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kharijites" title="Kharijites">Kharijites</a> who rebelled against the formation of the state; led their battles in the name of "the book of God (<i><a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Qur'an</a></i>) and the <i>Sunnah</i> of his Prophet".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Second_Fitna" title="Second Fitna">second Civil War</a> (680â92) the Sunna-term received connotations critical of <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shi'i</a> doctrines (<i>Tashayyu'</i>). It is recorded by MasrÅ«q ibn al-AdschdaÊ¿ (d. 683), who was a <i><a href="/wiki/Mufti" title="Mufti">Mufti</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Kufa" title="Kufa">Kufa</a>, a need to love the first two caliphs <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Bakr" title="AbÅ« Bakr">AbÅ« Bakr</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CA%BFUmar_ibn_al-Kha%E1%B9%AD%E1%B9%AD%C4%81b" title="Ê¿Umar ibn al-Khaá¹­á¹­Äb">Ê¿Umar ibn al-Khaá¹­á¹­Äb</a> and acknowledge their priority (FadÄ'il). A disciple of MasrÅ«q, the scholar ash-ShaÊ¿bÄ« (d. between 721 und 729), who first sided with the Shia in Kufa during Civil War, but turned away in disgust by their fanaticism and finally decided to join the umayyad Caliph <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Malik_ibn_Marwan" title="Abd al-Malik ibn Marwan">Ê¿Abd al-Malik</a>, popularized the concept of <i>sunnah</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> It is also passed down by asch-ShaÊ¿bÄ«, that he took offensive at the hatred on <a href="/wiki/Aisha" title="Aisha">Ê¿ÄÊ¾iÅ¡a bint AbÄ« Bakr</a> and considered it a violation of the <i><a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i>Sunna</i> instead of the longer expression <i>ahl as-sunna</i> or <i>ahl as-sunna wa-l-Ç§amÄÊ¿a</i> as a group-name for Sunnis is a relatively young phenomenon. It was probably <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Taymiyyah" title="Ibn Taymiyyah">Ibn Taymiyyah</a>, who used the short-term for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> It was later popularized by <a href="/wiki/Pan-Islamism" title="Pan-Islamism">pan-Islamic</a> scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Rashid_Rida" title="Rashid Rida">Muhammad Rashid Rida</a> in his treatise <i>as-Sunna wa-Å¡-Å¡iÊ¿a au al-WahhÄbÄ«ya wa-r-RÄfiá¸a: á¸¤aqÄÊ¾iq dÄ«nÄ«ya taÊ¾rÄ«á¸«Ä«ya iÇ§timaÊ¿Ä«ya iá¹£laá¸¥Ä«ya</i> ("The Sunna and the Shia, Or <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabism</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Rafida" title="Rafida">RÄfidism</a></i>: religious history, sociological und reformoriented factsâ) published in 1928â29.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The term "<i>sunna</i>" is usually used in Arabic discourse as designation for Sunni Muslims, when they are intended to be contrasted with Shias. The word pair "Sunnah-Shia" is also used on Western research literature to denote the Sunni-Shia contrast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ahl_as-Sunna">Ahl as-Sunna</span></h3>
<p>One of the earliest supporting documents for <i>ahl as-sunna</i> derives from the Basric scholar Muhammad Ibn Siri (d. 728). His is mentioned in the <i>Sahih</i> of <a href="/wiki/Muslim_ibn_al-Hajjaj" title="Muslim ibn al-Hajjaj">Muslim ibn al-Hajjaj</a> quoted with: "Formerly one did not aksed about the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isnad" title="Isnad">Isnad</a>. But when the <i><a href="/wiki/Fitna_(word)" title="Fitna (word)">fitna</a></i> started, one said: 'Name us your informants'. One would then respond to them: if they were Sunnah people, you accept their hadith. But if they are people of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bid%CA%BBah" title="BidÊ»ah">Innovations</a>, the hadith was rejected."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> G.H.A. Juynboll assumed, the term <i>fitna</i> in this statement is not related to the first Civil War (665â661) after murder of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CA%BFUthm%C4%81n_ibn_%CA%BFAff%C4%81n" title="Ê¿UthmÄn ibn Ê¿AffÄn">Ê¿UthmÄn ibn Ê¿AffÄn</a>, but the second Civil War (680â692)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> in which the Islamic community was split into four parties (<a href="/wiki/Abd_Allah_ibn_al-Zubayr" title="Abd Allah ibn al-Zubayr">Abd Allah ibn al-Zubayr</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_dynasty" title="Umayyad dynasty">Umayyads</a>, the Shia under <a href="/wiki/Mukhtar_al-Thaqafi" title="Mukhtar al-Thaqafi">al-MukhtÄr ibn AbÄ« Ê¿Ubaid</a> and the Kharijites). The term <i>ahl sunna</i> designated in this situation whose, who stayed away from heretic teachings of the different warring parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i>ahl as-sunna</i> was always a laudatory designation. <a href="/wiki/Abu_Hanifa" title="Abu Hanifa">Abu Hanifa</a> (d. 769), who sympathized with <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Murdshia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Murdshia (page does not exist)">Murdshia</a>, insisted that this were "righteous people and people of the Sunnah" (<i>ahl al-Ê¿adl wa-ahl as-sunna</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>  According to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Josef_van_Ess&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Josef van Ess (page does not exist)">Josef van Ess</a> this term did not mean more than "honorable and righteous believing people".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Among Hanafits the designation <i>ahl as-sunna</i> and <i>ahl al-Ê¿adl</i> (people of the righteous) remained interchangeable for a long time. Thus the Hanafite AbÅ« l-QÄsim as-SamarqandÄ« (d. 953), who composed a catechism for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samanides" title="Samanides">Samanides</a>, used sometimes one expression and sometimes another for his own group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudolph_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rudolph-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Singular to <i>ahl as-sunna</i> was <i>á¹£Äá¸¥ib sunna</i> (adherent to the sunnah).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> This expression was used for example by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Mubarak" title="Ibn al-Mubarak">Ê¿AbdallÄh ibn al-MubÄrak</a> (d. 797) for a person, who distances himself from the teachings of shia, kharijites, qadarites and murdajites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In addition, the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_nouns_and_adjectives#Nisba" title="Arabic nouns and adjectives">Nisba</a> adjective <i>sunnÄ«</i> was also used for the individual person. Thus it has been recorded, the Kufic scholar of the Quran Abu Bakr ibn'auyash (d. 809) was asked, how he was a "sunni". He responded the following: âThe one who, when the heresies are mentioned, doesn't get excited about any of them.â<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>  The andalisian scholar <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hazm" title="Ibn Hazm">ibn Hazm</a> (d. 1064) taught later, that whose who confess to Islam can be divided into four groups: <i>ahl as-sunna</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutazilites" title="Mutazilites">Mutazilites</a>, Murdschiiten, Schiiten und Charidschiten.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The MuÊ¿tazilites replaced the Qadarites here.
</p><p>In the 9th Century, one started to extent the term <i>ahl as-sunna</i> with further positive additions. Abu l-Hasan al-Ashari used for his own group expressions like <i>ahl as-sunna wa-l-istiqÄma</i> (âpeople of Sunna and Straightnessâ), <i>ahl as-sunna wa-l-á¸¥adÄ«á¹¯</i> (âpeople of Sunnah and of the Hadithâ)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> or <i>ahl al-á¸¥aqq wa-s-sunna</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kitab_al-Ibana8_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kitab_al-Ibana8-30">[30]</a></sup> (âpeople of truth and of the Sunnahâ).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ahl_as-Sunna_wa-l-jama">Ahl as-Sunna wa-l-jama</span></h3>
<p>When the expression 'ahl as-sunna wal-jama' appeared for the first time, is not entirely clear. Der Abbasite Caliph <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27m%C5%ABn" title="Al-Ma'mÅ«n">Al-Ma'mÅ«n</a> (reigned 813â33) criticized in his Miha-edict a group of people, who related themselves to the sunnah (<i>nasabÅ« anfusa-hum ilÄ s-sunna</i>) and claimed, they are the "people of truth, religion and community" (<i>ahl al-á¸¥aqq wa-d-dÄ«n wa-l-Ç§amÄÊ¿a</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> <i>Sunna</i> and <i>jama</i> are already connected here. As a pair, these terms already appear in the 9th Century. It is recorded that the disciple of Ahmad ibn Hanbal Harb ibn Ismail as Sirjdshani (d. 893) created a writing with the title <i>as-Sunna wa-l-Ç§amÄÊ¿a</i>, to which the Mutazilite <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Abu_l-Qasim_al-Balchi&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Abu l-Qasim al-Balchi (page does not exist)">Abu l-Qasim al-Balchi</a> wrote a refutation later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al-Jubba%27i" title="Al-Jubba'i">Al-Jubba'i</a> (d. 916) tells in his <i>KitÄb al-MaqÄlÄt</i>, that Ahmad ibn Hanbal attributed to his students the predicate <i>sunnÄ« jama</i> (âJammatic Sunniteâ).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> This indicates that the Hanbalis were the first to use the phrase <i>ahl as-sunna wal-Ç§amÄÊ¿a</i> as a self-designation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ess20111276_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ess20111276-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>But also the <a href="/wiki/Karramiyya" title="Karramiyya">Karramiyya</a> founded by Muhammad ibn Karram (d. 859)  referred to the sunnah and community. They passed down for praise of their schoolfounder a hadith, according to which the prophet Muhammad predicted that at the end of times a man named Muhammad ibn Karram will appear, who will restore the sunna and the community (<i>as-sunna wal-Ç§amÄÊ¿a</i>) and take Hidraj from Chorasan to Jerusalem, just how Muhammad himself took a Hidraj from Mecca to Medina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ess20111276_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ess20111276-34">[34]</a></sup> According to the testimony of the transoxanian scholar Abu l-Yusr al-Bazdawi (d. 1099) the Kullabites (followers of the Basrian scholar ibn Kullab (d. 855)) dayed about themselves, that they are among the <i>ahl as-sunna wal-jama</i> too.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bazdawi250_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bazdawi250-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Abu l-Hasan al-Ashari used the expression <i>ahl as-sunna wal-jama</i> rarely,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> and preferred another combination. Later Asharites like al-Isfaranini (d. 1027) nad Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi (d. 1078) used the expression <i>ahl as-sunna wal-jama</i> too and used them in their works to designate the teachings of their own school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> According to al-Bazdawi all Asharites in his time said they belong to the <i>ahl as-sunna wal-jama</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bazdawi250_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bazdawi250-35">[35]</a></sup> During this time, the term has been used as a self-designation by the hanafite Maturidites in Transoxiania, used frequently by <a href="/wiki/Abu_al-Layth_al-Samarqandi" title="Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi">Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi</a> (d. 983), Abu Schakur as Salimi (d. 1086) and al-Bazdawi himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rudolph_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rudolph-24">[24]</a></sup> They used the term as a contrast from their enemies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> among them Hanafites in the West, who have been followers of the Mutazilites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Al-BazdawÄ« also contrasted the <i>ahl as-sunna wal-jama'</i> with <i>ahl-al-á¸¥adÄ«á¹¯</i>, "because they would adhere to teachings contrary to the Quran".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Muqaddas%C4%AB" title="Al-MuqaddasÄ«">Schams ad-DÄ«n al-MaqdisÄ«</a> (end of the 10the Century) was the expression <i>ahl as-sunna wal-jama</i> a laudatory term during his time, similar to <i>ahl al-Ê¿adl wa-t-tauá¸¥Ä«d</i> (âpeople of righteousness and Divine Unityâ), which was used for Mutazilites or generally designations like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mu%27min" title="Mu'min">Mu'min</a>Å«n (âBelieverâ) oder <i>aá¹£á¸¥Äb al-hudÄ</i> (âpeople of guidanceâ) for Muslims, who has been seen as rightoues believers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Since the expression <i>ahl as-sunna wa-l-jama</i> was used with a demand on rightoues belief, it was used in academic researches translated as "orthodox".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are different opinions regarding what the term <i>jama</i> in the phrase <i>ahl as-sunna wal-jama</i> actually means, among Muslim scholars. In the Sunni Creed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/At-Tahawi" title="At-Tahawi">at-Tahawi</a> (d. 933), the term <i>jama</i> contrasts several times the Arabic term <i>furqa</i> ("Division, sectarianism").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Thus at-TahÄwÄ« explains that <i>jama</i> is considered as true or right (<i>á¸¥aqq wa-á¹£awÄb</i>) and <i>furqa</i> as aberration and punishment (<i>zaiÄ¡ wa-Ê¿aá¸Äb</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Ibn Taymiyyah argues, that <i>jama</i> as opposite term to <i>furqa</i> inherents the meaning of <i>iÇ§timÄÊ¿</i> ("Coming together, being together, agreement"). Furthermore he connects it with the principle of <a href="/wiki/Ijma" title="Ijma">Ijma</a>, a third juridical source after the Book (= Quran), and the Sunnah.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The Ottoman scholar Muslih ad Din al-Qastallani (d. 1495) held the opinnion that <i>jama</i> means âPath of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahaba" title="Sahaba">Sahaba</a>â (<i>á¹­arÄ«qat aá¹£-á¹£aá¸¥Äba</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zabidi6_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zabidi6-47">[47]</a></sup> The modern Indonesian theologican <a href="/wiki/Nurcholish_Madjid" title="Nurcholish Madjid">Nurcholish Madjid</a> (d. 2005) interpreted <i>jama</i> as an <a href="/wiki/Inclusivism" title="Inclusivism">inclusivistic</a> concept: It means a society open for <a href="/wiki/Value_pluralism" title="Value pluralism">pluralism</a> and dialogue but does not emphasize that much.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kaaba_daylight.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="588" data-file-width="847" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Kaaba_daylight.png/220px-Kaaba_daylight.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Kaaba_daylight.png/330px-Kaaba_daylight.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Kaaba_daylight.png/440px-Kaaba_daylight.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kaaba_daylight.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a> mosque in <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> is the largest and most important <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosque</a> in the world.</div></div></div>
<p>One common mistake is to assume that Sunni Islam represents a normative Islam that emerged during the period after Muhammad's death, and that <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shi%27ism" title="Shi'ism">Shi'ism</a> developed out of Sunni Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> This perception is partly due to the reliance on highly ideological sources that have been accepted as reliable historical works, and also because the vast majority of the population is Sunni. Both Sunnism and Shiaism are the end products of several centuries of competition between ideologies. Both sects used each other to further cement their own identities and doctrines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first four caliphs are known among Sunnis as the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun" title="Rashidun">RÄshidun</a> or "Rightly-Guided Ones". Sunni recognition includes the aforementioned <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a> as the first, <a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Umar</a> as the second, <a href="/wiki/Uthman" title="Uthman">Uthman</a> as the third, and <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a> as the fourth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Sunnis recognised different rulers as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">caliph</a>, though they did not include anyone in the list of the rightly guided ones or <i>RÄshidun</i> after the murder of Ali, until the caliphate was constitutionally abolished in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> on 3 March 1924.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transition_of_caliphate_into_dynastic_monarchy_of_Banu_Umayya">Transition of caliphate into dynastic monarchy of Banu Umayya</span></h3>
<p>The seeds of metamorphosis of caliphate into kingship were sown, as the second caliph Umar had feared, as early as the regime of the third caliph Uthman, who appointed many of his kinsmen from his clan <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banu_Umayya" title="Banu Umayya">Banu Umayya</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Marwan_I" title="Marwan I">MarwÄn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Walid_ibn_Uqba" title="Al-Walid ibn Uqba">Walid bin Uqba</a> on important government positions, becoming the main cause of turmoil resulting in his murder and the ensuing infighting during Ali's time and rebellion by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muawiyah_I" title="Muawiyah I">MuÄwiya</a>, another of Uthman's kinsman. This ultimately resulted in the establishment of firm dynastic rule of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banu_Umayya" title="Banu Umayya">Banu Umayya</a> after <a href="/wiki/Husayn_ibn_Ali" title="Husayn ibn Ali">Husain</a>, the younger son of Ali from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatima_bint_Muhammad" title="Fatima bint Muhammad">FÄtima</a>,  was killed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Karbala" title="Battle of Karbala">Battle of KarbalÄ</a>. The rise to power of Banu Umayya, the Meccan tribe of elites who had vehemently opposed Muhammad under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Abu_Sufyan_ibn_Harb" title="Abu Sufyan ibn Harb">Abu SufyÄn</a>, MuÄwiya's father, right up to the <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_Mecca" title="Conquest of Mecca">conquest of Mecca</a> by Muhammad, as his successors  with the accession of Uthman to caliphate, replaced the egalitarian society formed as a result of Muhammad's revolution to a society stratified between haves and have-nots as a result of <a href="/wiki/Nepotism" title="Nepotism">nepotism</a>, and in the words of El-Hibri through "the use of religious charity revenues (<i><a href="/wiki/Zakat" title="Zakat">zakÄt</a></i>) to subsidise family interests, which Uthman justified as '<i>al-sila</i>' (pious filial support)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Ali, during his rather brief regime after Uthman maintained austere life style and tried hard to bring back the egalitarian system and supremacy of law over the ruler idealised in Muhammad's message, but faced continued opposition, and wars one after another by <a href="/wiki/Aisha" title="Aisha">Aisha</a>-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Talhah#Battle_of_the_Camel_and_Death" title="Talhah">Talhah</a>-Zubair, by MuÄwiya and finally by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kharjites" title="Kharjites">KhÄrjites</a>. After he was murdered his followers immediately elected <a href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_Ali" title="Hasan ibn Ali">Hasan ibn Ali</a> his elder son from FÄtima to succeed him. Hasan shortly afterward signed a treaty with MuÄwiya relinquishing power in favour of the latter, with a condition inter alia, that one of the two who will outlive the other will be the caliph, and that this caliph will not appoint a successor but will leave the matter of selection of the caliph to the public. Subsequently, Hasan was poisoned to death and Muawiya enjoyed unchallenged power. Dishonouring his treaty with Hasan, he nominated his son <a href="/wiki/Yazid_I" title="Yazid I">Yazid</a> to succeed him. Upon MuÄwiya's death, Yazid asked Husain, the younger brother of Hasan, Ali's son and Muhammad's grandson, to give his allegiance to Yazid, which he plainly refused. His caravan was cordoned by Yazid's army at KarbalÄ and he was killed with all his male companions â total 72 people, in a day long <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Karbala" title="Battle of Karbala">battle</a> after which Yazid established himself as a sovereign, though strong public uprising erupted after his death against his dynasty to avenge the massacre of KarbalÄ, but Banu Umayya were able to quickly suppress them all and ruled the Muslim world, till they were finally overthrown by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banu_Abbas" title="Banu Abbas">Banu AbbÄs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Caliphate_and_the_dynastic_monarchy_of_Banu_Abb.C4.81s"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Caliphate_and_the_dynastic_monarchy_of_Banu_AbbÄs">Caliphate and the dynastic monarchy of Banu AbbÄs</span></h3>
<p>The rule of and "caliphate" of Banu Umayya came to an end at the hands of Banu AbbÄs a branch of Banu HÄshim, the tribe of Muhammad, only to usher another dynastic monarchy styled as caliphate from 750 CE. This period is seen formative in Sunni Islam as the founders of the four schools viz, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Hanifa" title="Abu Hanifa">Abu Hanifa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malik_ibn_Anas" title="Malik ibn Anas">Malik ibn Anas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shafi%27i" title="Shafi'i">ShÄfi'i</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Hanbal" title="Ahmad ibn Hanbal">Ahmad bin Hanbal</a> all practised during this time, so also did <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jafar_al_Sadiq" title="Jafar al Sadiq">Jafar al SÄdiq</a> who elaborated the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Imamate" title="Imamate">imÄmate</a>, the basis for the Shi'a religious thought. There was no clearly accepted formula for determining succession in the Abbasid caliphate. Two or three sons or other relatives of the dying caliph emerged as candidates to the throne, each supported by his own party of supporters. A trial of strength ensued and the most powerful party won and expected favours of the caliph they supported once he ascended the throne. The caliphate of this dynasty ended with the death of the Caliph al-Ma'mun in 833 CE, when the period of Turkish domination began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sunni_Islam_in_the_contemporary_era">Sunni Islam in the contemporary era</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SA_Blue_Mosque.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/SA_Blue_Mosque.jpg/220px-SA_Blue_Mosque.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/SA_Blue_Mosque.jpg/330px-SA_Blue_Mosque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/SA_Blue_Mosque.jpg/440px-SA_Blue_Mosque.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SA_Blue_Mosque.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sultan Salahuddin Abdul Aziz Mosque in Shah Alam, <a href="/wiki/Selangor" title="Selangor">Selangor</a>, Malaysia.</div></div></div>
<p>The fall, at the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, the biggest Sunni empire for six centuries, brought the caliphate to an end. This resulted in Sunni protests in far off places including the <a href="/wiki/Khilafat_Movement" title="Khilafat Movement">Khilafat Movement</a> in India, which was later on upon gaining independence from Britain divided into Sunni dominated <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and secular <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. Pakistan, the most populous Sunni state at its dawn, was later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bangladesh_Independence_War" title="Bangladesh Independence War">partitioned</a> into Pakistan and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Abolition_of_the_Caliphate" title="Abolition of the Caliphate">demise of Ottoman caliphate</a> also resulted in the emergence of <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, a dynastic absolute monarchy that championed the reformist doctrines of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Abd_al-Wahhab" title="Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab">Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahhab</a>; the eponym of the <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabi movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> This was followed by a considerable rise in the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabi</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Salafi_movement" title="Salafi movement">Salafiyya</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jihadism" title="Jihadism">Jihadist</a> movements that revived the doctrines of the Hanbali theologian <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Taymiyyah" title="Ibn Taymiyyah">Taqi Al-Din Ibn Taymiyyah</a> (1263â1328 C.E/ 661â728 A.H), a fervent advocate of the traditions of the Sunni Imam <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Hanbal" title="Ahmad ibn Hanbal">Ahmad ibn Hanbal</a>. The expediencies of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cold_war" title="Cold war">cold war</a> resulted in the radicalisation of Afghan refugees in Pakistan who fought the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communist" title="Communist">communist</a> regime backed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR">USSR</a> forces in Afghanistan giving birth to the <a href="/wiki/Taliban" title="Taliban">Taliban movement</a>. After the fall of communist regime in Afghanistan and the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Afghan_Civil_War_(1992%E2%80%931996)" title="Afghan Civil War (1992â1996)">civil war</a>, Taliban wrestled power from the various <a href="/wiki/Afghan_mujahideen" title="Afghan mujahideen">Mujahidin factions</a> in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and formed a government under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Mullah_Omar" title="Mullah Omar">Mohammed Omar</a>, who was addressed as the <a href="/wiki/Emir" title="Emir">Emir</a> of the faithful, an honorific way of addressing the caliph. The Taliban regime was recognised by Pakistan and Saudi Arabia till after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/9/11" title="9/11">9/11</a>, perpetrated by <a href="/wiki/Osama_bin_Laden" title="Osama bin Laden">Osama bin Laden</a> â a Saudi national by birth and harboured by the Taliban â took place, resulting in a <a href="/wiki/War_on_terror" title="War on terror">war on terror</a> launched against the Taliban.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>The sequence of events of the 20th century has led to resentment in some quarters of the Sunni community due to the loss of pre-eminence in several previously Sunni-dominated regions such as the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>, the <a href="/wiki/North_Caucasus" title="North Caucasus">North Caucasus</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_sub_continent" title="Indian sub continent">Indian sub continent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The latest attempt by a radical wing of <a href="/wiki/Salafi_jihadism" title="Salafi jihadism">Salafi-Jihadists</a> to re-establish a Sunni caliphate was seen in the emergence of the militant group <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">ISIL</a>, whose leader <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr_al-Baghdadi" title="Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi">Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi</a> is known among his followers as caliph and <i>Amir-al-mu'mineen</i>, "The Commander of the Faithful".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Jihadism is opposed from within the Muslim community (known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">ummah</a></i> in Arabic) in all quarters of the world as evidenced by turnout of almost 2% of the Muslim population in London protesting against ISIL.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the puritan approach of <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Kathir" title="Ibn Kathir">Ibn Kathir</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Rashid_Rida" title="Muhammad Rashid Rida">Muhammad Rashid Rida</a>, etc. many contemporary <i><a href="/wiki/Tafsir" title="Tafsir">Tafsir</a></i> (exegetic treatises) downplay the earlier significance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isra%27iliyyat" title="Isra'iliyyat">Biblical material</a> (<i>IsrÄ'iliyyÄt</i>). Half of the Arab commentaries reject <i>IsrÄ'iliyyÄt</i> in general, while Turkish tafsir usually partly allow referring to Biblical material. Nevertheless, most non-Arabic commentators regard them as useless or not applicable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pink_pp_114-116_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pink_pp_114-116-70">[70]</a></sup> A direct reference to the <a href="/wiki/Israeli%E2%80%93Palestinian_conflict" title="IsraeliâPalestinian conflict">IsraeliâPalestinian conflict</a> could not be found. It remains unclear whether the refusal of <i>IsrÄ'iliyyÄt</i> is motivated by political discourse or by traditionalist thought alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pink_pp_114-116_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pink_pp_114-116-70">[70]</a></sup> The usage of <i>tafsir'ilmi</i> is another notable characteristic of modern Sunni tafsir. <i>Tafsir'ilmi</i> stands for alleged scientific miracles found in the Qur'an. In short, the idea is that the Qur'an contains knowledge about subjects an author of the 7th century could not possibly have. Such interpretations are popular among many commentators. Some scholars, such as the Commentators of <a href="/wiki/Al-Azhar_University" title="Al-Azhar University">Al-Azhar University</a>, reject this approach, arguing the Qur'an is a text for religious guidance, not for science and scientific theories that may be disproved later; thus <i>tafsir'ilmi</i> might lead to interpreting Qur'anic passages as falsehoods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pink_pp_120-121_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pink_pp_120-121-71">[71]</a></sup> Modern trends of Islamic interpretation are usually seen as adjusting to a modern audience and purifying Islam from alleged alterings, some of which are believed to be intentional corruptions brought into Islam to undermine and corrupt its message.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pink_pp_114-116_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pink_pp_114-116-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Adherents">Adherents</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Islam_by_country.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="922" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Islam_by_country.png/260px-Islam_by_country.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Islam_by_country.png/390px-Islam_by_country.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Islam_by_country.png/520px-Islam_by_country.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Islam_by_country.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries with more than 95% Muslim population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#4a6600; color:white;">Â </span>Â <b>Sunni</b></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#66004a; color:white;">Â </span>Â <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shias</a></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#04161b; color:white;">Â </span>Â <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibadi" title="Ibadi">Ibadi</a></div></div></div></div>
<p>Sunnis believe the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahabah" title="Sahabah">companions</a> of Muhammad to be reliable transmitters of Islam, since God and Muhammad accepted their integrity. Medieval sources even prohibit cursing or vilifying them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> This belief is based upon prophetic traditions such as one narrated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdullah,_son_of_Masud" title="Abdullah, son of Masud">Abdullah, son of Masud</a>, in which Muhammad said: "The best of the people are my generation, then those who come after them, then those who come after them." Support for this view is also found in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Qur'an</a>, according to Sunnis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Therefore, <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">narratives</a> of companions are also reliably taken into account for knowledge of the Islamic faith. Sunnis also believe that the companions were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mu%27min" title="Mu'min">true believers</a> since it was the companions who were given the task of <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Quran#Collection_of_the_Quran" title="History of the Quran">compiling the Qur'an</a>.
</p><p>Sunni Islam does not have a formal hierarchy. Leaders are informal, and gain influence through study to become a scholar of Islamic law (<i><a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a></i>) or Islamic theology (<i><a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">KalÄm</a></i>). Both religious and political leadership are in principle open to all Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> According to the Islamic Center of <a href="/wiki/Columbia,_South_Carolina" title="Columbia, South Carolina">Columbia</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>, anyone with the intelligence and the will can become an Islamic scholar. During Midday Mosque services on Fridays, the congregation will choose a well-educated person to lead the service, known as a Khateeb (one who speaks).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>A study conducted by the <i><a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a></i> in 2010 and released January 2011<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pewmuslim2_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-pewmuslim2-77">[77]</a></sup> found that there are 1.62Â billion Muslims around the world, and it is estimated over 85â90% are Sunni.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sunni_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sunni-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Three_group_doctrines">Three group doctrines</span></h3>
<p>Regarding the question which dogmatic tendencies are to be assigned to Sunnism, there is no agreement among Muslim scholars. Since the early modern period, is the idea that a total of three groups belong to the Sunnis: 1. those named after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Ash%27ari" title="Al-Ash'ari">Abu l-Hasan al-AschÊ¿ari</a> (d. 935) <a href="/wiki/Ash%27ari" title="Ash'ari">AshÊ¿arites</a>, 2. those named after <a href="/wiki/Abu_Mansur_al-Maturidi" title="Abu Mansur al-Maturidi">Abu Mansur al-Maturidi</a> (d. 941) named <a href="/wiki/Maturidi" title="Maturidi">Maturidites</a> and 3. a differently named third group, which is traditionalistic-oriented and rejects the rational discourse of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kal%C4%81m" title="KalÄm">KalÄm</a> advocated by the Maturidites and AshÊ¿arites. The Syrian scholar Ê¿Abd al-Baqi Ibn Faqih Fussa (d. 1661) calls this third traditionalist group the Hanbalites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The late Ottoman thinker Ä°smail HakkÄ± Ä°zmirli (d. 1946), who agreed to dividing Sunnis into these three groups, called the traditionalist group <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salafism" title="Salafism">Salafiyya</a>, but also used <i>Athariyya</i> as an alternative term. For the Maturidiyya he gives <i>NasafÄ«ya</i> as a possible alternative name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IsmailHakki75_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-IsmailHakki75-80">[80]</a></sup> Another used for the traditionalist-oriented group is "people of <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a>" (<i>ahl al-á¸¥adÄ«á¹¯</i>). It is used, for example, in the final document of the Grozny Conference. Only those "people of the Hadith" are assigned to Sunnism who practice <i>tafwÄ«á¸</i>, i.e. who refrain from interpreting the ambiguous statements of the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grosny_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grosny-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Ash.27ari"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ash'ari">Ash'ari</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ash%27ari" title="Ash'ari">Ash'ari</a></div>
<p>Founded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_al-Hasan_al-Ash%27ari" title="Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari">Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari</a> (873â935). This theological school of Aqeedah was embraced by many Muslim scholars and developed in parts of the Islamic world throughout history; <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">al-Ghazali</a> wrote on the creed discussing it and agreeing upon some of its principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ash'ari theology stresses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divine_revelation" title="Divine revelation">divine revelation</a> over human reason. Contrary to the Mu'tazilites, they say that <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a> cannot be derived from human reason, but that God's commands, as revealed in the <i>Quran</i> and the <i>Sunnah</i> (the practices of Muhammad and his companions as recorded in the traditions, or <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a>), are the sole source of all morality and ethics.
</p><p>Regarding the nature of God and the divine attributes, the Ash'ari rejected the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mu%27tazili" title="Mu'tazili">Mu'tazili</a> position that all Quranic references to God as having real attributes were metaphorical. The Ash'aris insisted that these attributes were as they "best befit His Majesty". The Arabic language is a wide language in which one word can have 15 different meanings, so the Ash'aris endeavor to find the meaning that best befits God and is not contradicted by the Quran. Therefore, when God states in the Quran, "He who does not resemble any of His creation", this clearly means that God cannot be attributed with body parts because He created body parts. Ash'aris tend to stress divine <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">omnipotence</a> over human free will and they believe that the Quran is eternal and uncreated.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Maturidi">Maturidi</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Maturidi" title="Maturidi">Maturidi</a></div>
<p>Founded by <a href="/wiki/Abu_Mansur_al-Maturidi" title="Abu Mansur al-Maturidi">Abu Mansur al-Maturidi</a> (d. 944), the Maturidiyyah was the major tradition in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> based on <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a>-law. It is more influenced by Persian interpretations of Islam and less on the traditions established within Arabian culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> In contrast to the traditionalistic approach, Maturidism allows to reject <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadiths</a> based on reason alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Nevertheless, revelation remains important to inform humans about that is beyond their intellectual limits, such as the concept of an afterlife. <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">Ethics</a> on the other hand, do not need prophecy or revelation, but can be understood by reason alone. One of the tribes, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seljuk_Turks" title="Seljuk Turks">Seljuk Turks</a>, migrated to <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, where later the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> was established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Their preferred school of law achieved a new prominence throughout their whole empire although it continued to be followed almost exclusively by followers of the <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> school while followers of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shafi" title="Shafi">Shafi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maliki" title="Maliki">Maliki</a> schools within the empire followed the Ash'ari and Athari schools of thought. Thus, wherever can be found <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> followers, there can be found the <a href="/wiki/Maturidi" title="Maturidi">Maturidi</a> creed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditionalist">Traditionalist</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Athari" title="Athari">Athari</a></div>
<p>Traditionalist or Athari theology is a movement of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> scholars who reject rationalistic Islamic theology (<i><a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">kalam</a></i>) in favor of strict textualism in interpreting the <i><a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Qur'an</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The name derives from "tradition" in its technical sense as translation of the Arabic word <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a></i>. It is also sometimes referred to as <i>athari</i> as by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditionalist_Theology_(Islam)#Terminology" title="Traditionalist Theology (Islam)">several other names</a>.
</p><p>Adherents of traditionalist theology believe that the <i><a href="/wiki/Zahir_(Islam)" title="Zahir (Islam)">zahir</a></i> (literal, apparent) meaning of the <i>Qur'an</i> and the <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a> have sole authority in matters of belief and law; and that the use of rational disputation is forbidden even if it verifies the truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halverson-36_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halverson-36-90">[90]</a></sup> They engage in a literal reading of the <i>Qur'an</i>, as opposed to one engaged in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ta%27wil" title="Ta'wil">ta'wil</a></i> (metaphorical interpretation). They do not attempt to conceptualize the meanings of the <i>Qur'an</i> rationally, and believe that their realities should be consigned to God alone (<i><a href="/wiki/Tafwid" title="Tafwid">tafwid</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halverson-3637_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halverson-3637-91">[91]</a></sup> In essence, the text of the Qur'an and Hadith is accepted without asking "how" or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bi-la_kaifa" title="Bi-la kaifa">Bi-la kaifa</a>".
</p><p>Traditionalist theology emerged among scholars of hadith who eventually coalesced into a movement called <i><a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Hadith" title="Ahl al-Hadith">ahl al-hadith</a></i> under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Hanbal" title="Ahmad ibn Hanbal">Ahmad ibn Hanbal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lapidus130_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lapidus130-92">[92]</a></sup> In matters of faith, they were pitted against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mu%27tazilites" title="Mu'tazilites">Mu'tazilites</a> and other theological currents, condemning many points of their doctrine as well as the rationalistic methods they used in defending them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lapidus130_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lapidus130-92">[92]</a></sup> In the tenth century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Ash%27ari" title="Al-Ash'ari">al-Ash'ari</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Maturidi" title="Al-Maturidi">al-Maturidi</a> found a middle ground between Mu'tazilite rationalism and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hanbalite" title="Hanbalite">Hanbalite</a> literalism, using the rationalistic methods championed by Mu'tazilites to defend most tenets of the traditionalist doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Although the mainly Hanbali scholars who rejected this synthesis were in the minority, their emotive, narrative-based approach to faith remained influential among the urban masses in some areas, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid</a> <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>While <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ash%27arism" title="Ash'arism">Ash'arism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maturidism" title="Maturidism">Maturidism</a> are often called the Sunni "orthodoxy", traditionalist theology has thrived alongside it, laying rival claims to be the orthodox Sunni faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> In the modern era, it has had a disproportionate impact on Islamic theology, having been appropriated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wahhabi" title="Wahhabi">Wahhabi</a> and other traditionalist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salafi" title="Salafi">Salafi</a> currents and have spread well beyond the confines of the <a href="/wiki/Hanbali" title="Hanbali">Hanbali</a> school of law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Narrow_definition">Narrow definition</span></h3>
<p>There were also Muslim scholars who wanted to limit the Sunni term to the <i><a href="/wiki/Ash%27ari" title="Ash'ari">Ash'arites</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Maturidi" title="Maturidi">MÄturÄ«dites</a></i> alone. For example, MurtadÄ az-ZabÄ«dÄ« (d. 1790) wrote in his commentary on <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">al-Ghazalis</a> "Iá¸¥yÄÊ¾ Ê¿ulÅ«m ad-dÄ«n": "When (sc. The term)" <i>ahl as-sunna wal jamaÊ¿a</i> is used, the AshÊ¿arites and MÄturÄ«dites are meant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zabidi6_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zabidi6-47">[47]</a></sup> This position was also taken over by the Egyptian Fatwa Office in July 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daralifta2366_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daralifta2366-98">[98]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> times, many efforts were made to establish a good harmony between the teachings of the AshÊ¿arÄ«ya and the MÄturÄ«dÄ«ya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daralifta2366_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Daralifta2366-98">[98]</a></sup> Finally, there were also scholars who regarded the AshÊ¿arites alone as Sunnis. For example, the Moroccan Sufi Ahmad ibn Ê¿Adschiba (d. 1809) stated in his commentary on <a href="/wiki/Al-Fatiha" title="Al-Fatiha">Fatiha</a>: âAs far as the Sunnis are concerned, it is the AshÊ¿arites and those who follow in their correct belief.â<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Conversely, there were also scholars who excluded the AshÊ¿arites from Sunnism. The Andalusian scholar <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hazm" title="Ibn Hazm">Ibn Hazm</a> (d. 1064) said that Abu l-Hasan al-AshÊ¿arÄ« belonged to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Murji%27a" title="Murji'a">Murji'a</a>, namely those who were particularly far removed from the Sunnis in terms of faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ibn_Hazm265f_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ibn_Hazm265f-100">[100]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Syrians" title="Syrians">Syrian</a>-<a href="/wiki/Albanians" title="Albanians">Albanian</a> Athari theologian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Nasiruddin_al-Albani" title="Muhammad Nasiruddin al-Albani">Muhammad Nasir al-Din al-Albani</a> rejected extremism in excluding <a href="/wiki/Ash%27ari" title="Ash'ari">Ash'aris</a> from Sunni Islam. He believed that despite that their fundamental differences from Atharis, not every Ash'ari is to be excluded from <i>Ahl al-Sunna wal Jama'ah</i>, unless they openly disapprove of the doctrines of the <i><a href="/wiki/Salaf" title="Salaf">Salaf</a></i> (<i>mad'hab as-Salaf</i>). According to Albani:</p><blockquote><p>"I do not share [the view of] some of the noble scholars of the past and present that we say about a group from the [many] Islamic groups that it is not from <i>Ahlus-Sunnah</i> due to its deviation in one issue or another...  as for whether the Ashâaris or the Maaturidis are from <i>Ahlus-Sunnah wal-Jamaaâah</i>, I say that they are from <i>Ahlus-Sunnah wal-Jamaaâah</i> in many things related to <i>aqidah</i> but in other <i><a href="/wiki/Aqidah" title="Aqidah">aqidah</a></i> issues they have deviated away from <i>Ahlus-Sunnah wal-Jamaaâah..</i> I donât hold that we should say that they are not from <i>Ahlus-Sunnah wal-Jamaaâah</i> whatsoever"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sunnism_in_general_and_in_a_specific_sense">Sunnism in general and in a specific sense</span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hanbalism" title="Hanbalism">Hanbali</a> scholar <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Taymiyyah" title="Ibn Taymiyyah">Ibn Taymiyyah</a> (d. 1328) distinguished in his work <i>MinhÄj as-sunna</i> between Sunnis in the general sense (<i>ahl as-unna al-Ê¿Ämma</i>) and Sunnis in the special sense (<i>ahl as-sunna al-á¸«Äá¹£á¹£a</i>). Sunnis in the general sense are all Muslims who recognize the caliphate of the three caliphs (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Bakr" title="AbÅ« Bakr">AbÅ« Bakr</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CA%BFUmar_ibn_al-Kha%E1%B9%AD%E1%B9%AD%C4%81b" title="Ê¿Umar ibn al-Khaá¹­á¹­Äb">Ê¿Umar ibn al-Khaá¹­á¹­Äb</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CA%BFUthm%C4%81n_ibn_%CA%BFAff%C4%81n" title="Ê¿UthmÄn ibn Ê¿AffÄn">Ê¿UthmÄn ibn Ê¿AffÄn</a>). In his opinion, this includes all Islamic groups except the Shiite <a href="/wiki/Rafida" title="Rafida">Rafidites</a>. Sunnis in the special sense are only the "people of the hadith" (<i>ahl al-á¸¥adÄ«á¹¯</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ä°smail HakkÄ± Ä°zmirli, who took over the distinction between a broader and narrower circle of Sunnis from Ibn Taimiya, said that <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Kullab" title="Ibn Kullab">Kullabiyya</a> and the AshÊ¿arÄ«yya are Sunnis in the general sense, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salafism" title="Salafism">Salafiyya</a> represent Sunnis in the specific sense. About the Maturidiyya he only says that they are closer to the Salafiyya than the AshÊ¿ariyya because they excel more in <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">Fiqh</a> than in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kal%C4%81m" title="KalÄm">KalÄm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IsmailHakki75_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-IsmailHakki75-80">[80]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi scholar</a> <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_al-Uthaymeen" title="Muhammad ibn al-Uthaymeen">Muhammad Ibn al-Ê¿Uthaimin</a> (d. 2001), who like Ibn Taimiya differentiated between Sunnis in general and special senses, also excluded the Asharites from the circle of Sunnis in the special sense and took the view that only the pious ancestors (<i>as-salaf aá¹£-á¹£Äliá¸¥</i>) who have agreed on the Sunnah belonged to this circle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ibn_Uthaimin_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ibn_Uthaimin-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Classification_of_the_Mu.CA.BFtazila"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_of_the_MuÊ¿tazila">Classification of the MuÊ¿tazila</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mu%CA%BFtazila" title="MuÊ¿tazila">MuÊ¿tazilites</a> are usually not regarded as Sunnis. <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hazm" title="Ibn Hazm">Ibn Hazm</a>, for example, contrasted them with the Sunnis as a separate group in his heresiographic work <i>al-Faá¹£l fi-l-milal wa-l-ahwÄÊ¾ wa-n-niá¸¥al</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ibn_Hazm265f_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ibn_Hazm265f-100">[100]</a></sup> In many medieval texts from the Islamic East, the <i>Ahl as-Sunna</i> are also differentiated to the MuÊ¿tazilites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> In 2010 the Jordanian <i><a href="/wiki/Fatwa" title="Fatwa">fatwa</a></i> office ruled out in a <a href="/wiki/Fatwa" title="Fatwa">fatwa</a> that the MuÊ¿tazilites, like the Kharijites, represent a doctrine that is contrary to Sunnism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Ibn Taymiyya argued that the MuÊ¿tazilites belong to the Sunnis in the general sense because they recognize the caliphate of the first three caliphs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mysticism">Mysticism</span></h3>
