<img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="215" data-file-width="206" decoding="async" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/27px-Allah-green.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/40px-Allah-green.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/54px-Allah-green.svg.png 2x" width="27"/>Â <a href="/wiki/Portal:Islam" title="Portal:Islam">Islam portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Sufism" title="Template:Sufism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Sufism" title="Template talk:Sufism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Sufism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>There is broad agreement that the <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufis</a> are also part of Sunnism. This view can already be found in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shafi%CA%BDi_school" title="ShafiÊ½i school">Shafi'ite</a> scholar <a href="/wiki/Abu_Mansur_al-Baghdadi" title="Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi">Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi</a> (d. 1037). In his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heresiography" title="Heresiography">heresiographical</a> work <i> al-Farq baina l-firaq </i> he divided the Sunnis into eight different categories (<i> aá¹£nÄf </i>) of people: 1. the theologians and <a href="/wiki/Kalam" title="Kalam">Kalam</a> Scholars, 2. the <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">Fiqh</a> scholars, 3. the traditional and <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a> scholars, 4. the <a href="/wiki/Adab_(Islam)" title="Adab (Islam)">Adab</a> and language scholars, 5. the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Koran" title="Koran">Koran</a> â Scholars, 6. the <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> ascetics (<i> az-zuhhÄd aá¹£-á¹£Å«fÄ«ya </i>), 7. those who perform the <i><a href="/wiki/Ribat" title="Ribat">ribat</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Jihad" title="Jihad">jihad</a></i> against the enemies of Islam, 8. the general crowd.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> According to this classification, the Sufis are one of a total of eight groups within Sunnism, defined according to their religious specialization.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisian</a> scholar Muhammad ibn al-QÄsim al-BakkÄ« (d. 1510) also included the Sufis in Sunnism. He divided the Sunnis into the following three groups according to their knowledge (<i> istiqrÄÊ¾ </i>):
</p>
<ol><li>the people of <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a> (<i> <a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Hadith" title="Ahl al-Hadith">ahl al-á¸¥adÄ«á¹¯h</a> </i>): Their principles are based on the hearing-based evidence, namely <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">the Book</a> (<i>Qur'an</i>), the <i><a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">Sunnah</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Ijma" title="Ijma">IjmÄÊ¿</a></i> (consensus).</li>
<li>The people of theory and the intellectual trade (<i> ahl an-naáºar wa-á¹£-á¹£inÄÊ¿a al-fikrÄ«ya </i>): They include the <a href="/wiki/Ash%27ari" title="Ash'ari">AshÊ¿arites</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafis</a>, the latter of whom consider <a href="/wiki/Abu_Mansur_al-Maturidi" title="Abu Mansur al-Maturidi">AbÅ« MansÅ«r al-MÄturÄ«dÄ«</a> as their master. They agree in the rational principles on all questions where there is no hearing-based evidence, in the hearing-based principles in everything that reason conceives as possible, and in the rational as well as the hearing-based principles in all other questions. They also agree on all dogmatic questions, except for the question of creation (<i> takwÄ«n </i>) and the question of <i><a href="/wiki/Taqlid" title="Taqlid">TaqlÄ«d</a></i>.</li>
<li>the people of feeling and revelation (<i> ahl al-wiÇ§dÄn wa-l-kaÅ¡f </i>): These are the <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufis</a>. Its principles correspond in the initial stage to the principles of the other two groups, but in the final stage they rely on revelation (<i>kaÅ¡f</i>) and inspiration (<i>ilhÄm</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></li></ol>
<p>Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Murtada_al-Zabidi" title="Murtada al-Zabidi">MurtadÄ az-ZabÄ«dÄ«</a> stated elsewhere in his commentary on <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Ghazzali's</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Revival_of_the_Religious_Sciences" title="The Revival of the Religious Sciences">Iá¸¥yÄÊ¾ Ê¿ulÅ«m ad-dÄ«n</a></i> <i> that the Sunnis consisted of four groups (firaq</i>), namely the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadith_scholar" title="Hadith scholar">hadith scholars</a> (<i>muá¸¥addiá¹¯hÅ«n), the Sufis, the AshÊ¿arites and the MÄturÄ«dites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></i>
</p><p>Some <i><a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">ulema</a></i> wanted to exclude the Sufis from Sunnism. The Yemeni scholar Ê¿AbbÄs ibn MansÅ«r as-SaksakÄ« (d. 1284) explained in his doxographic work <i> al-BurhÄn fÄ« maÊ¿rifat Ê¿aqÄÊ¾id ahl al-adyÄn </i> ("The evidence of knowledge of the beliefs of followers of different religions") about the Sufis: "They associate themselves with the Sunnis, but they do not belong to them, because they contradict them in their beliefs, actions and teachings." That is what distinguishes the Sufis from Sunnis according to as-SaksakÄ« their orientation to the hidden inner meaning of the <i>Qur'an</i> and the <i>Sunnah</i>. In this, he said, they resemble the <a href="/wiki/Batiniyya" title="Batiniyya">BÄtinites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> According to the final document of the Grozny Conference, only those Sufis are to be regarded as Sunnis who are "people of pure Sufism" (<i> ahl at-taá¹£auwuf aá¹£-á¹£ÄfÄ« </i>) in the knowledge, ethics and purification of the interior, according to <a href="/wiki/Tariqa" title="Tariqa">Method</a> as practiced by <a href="/wiki/Junayd_of_Baghdad" title="Junayd of Baghdad">al-Junaid Al- Baghdadi</a> and the "Imams of Guidance" (<i> aÊ¾imma al-hudÄ </i>) who followed his path.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grosny_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grosny-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the eleventh-century, Sufism, which had previously been a less "codified" trend in Islamic piety, began to be "ordered and crystallized"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seyyed_Hossein_Nasr_2007_p._76_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seyyed_Hossein_Nasr_2007_p._76-111">[111]</a></sup> into <i><a href="/wiki/Tariqa" title="Tariqa">Tariqahs</a></i> (orders) which have continued until the present day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seyyed_Hossein_Nasr_2007_p._76_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Seyyed_Hossein_Nasr_2007_p._76-111">[111]</a></sup> All these orders were founded by a major Sunni Islamic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_(Islamic)" title="Saint (Islamic)">saint</a>, and some of the largest and most widespread included the <a href="/wiki/Qadiriyya" title="Qadiriyya">Qadiriyya</a> (after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdul-Qadir_Gilani" title="Abdul-Qadir Gilani">Abdul-Qadir Gilani</a> [d. 1166]), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rifa%27i" title="Rifa'i">Rifa'iyya</a> (after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ahmed_al-Rifa%27i" title="Ahmed al-Rifa'i">Ahmed al-Rifa'i</a> [d. 1182]), the <a href="/wiki/Chishti_Order" title="Chishti Order">Chishtiyya</a> (after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moinuddin_Chishti" title="Moinuddin Chishti">Moinuddin Chishti</a> [d. 1236]), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shadiliyya" title="Shadiliyya">Shadiliyya</a> (after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abul_Hasan_ash-Shadhili" title="Abul Hasan ash-Shadhili">Abul Hasan ash-Shadhili</a> [d. 1258]), and the <a href="/wiki/Naqshbandi" title="Naqshbandi">Naqshbandiyya</a> (after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baha-ud-Din_Naqshband_Bukhari" title="Baha-ud-Din Naqshband Bukhari">Baha-ud-Din Naqshband Bukhari</a> [d. 1389]).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seyyed_Hossein_Nasr_2007_p._76_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-Seyyed_Hossein_Nasr_2007_p._76-111">[111]</a></sup> Contrary to popular <a href="/wiki/Orientalism" title="Orientalism">Orientalist</a> depictions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin_Lings_1983,_p.16_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martin_Lings_1983,_p.16-112">[112]</a></sup> neither the founders of these orders nor their followers considered themselves to be anything other than orthodox Sunni Muslims,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin_Lings_1983,_p.16_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-Martin_Lings_1983,_p.16-112">[112]</a></sup> Many of the most eminent defenders of Islamic orthodoxy, such as <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Qadir_Gilani" title="Abdul Qadir Gilani">'Abd al-Qadir Jilani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al-Ghazali</a>, Sultan á¹¢alÄá¸¥ ad-DÄ«n Al-Ayyubi (<a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">Saladin</a>) were connected with Sufism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salafi" title="Salafi">Salafi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wahhabi" title="Wahhabi">Wahhabi</a> strands of Sunnism do not accept many mystical practices associated with the contemporary Sufi orders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Jurisprudence">Jurisprudence</span></h2>
<p>Interpreting Islamic law by deriving specific rulings â such as how to pray â is commonly known as <a href="/wiki/Fiqh" title="Fiqh">Islamic jurisprudence</a>. The schools of law all have their own particular tradition of interpreting this jurisprudence. As these schools represent clearly spelled out methodologies for interpreting Islamic law, there has been little change in the methodology with regard to each school. While conflict between the schools was often violent in the past,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chib_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-chib-115">[115]</a></sup> the four Sunni schools recognize each other's validity and they have interacted in legal debate over the centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rabb_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-rabb-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hussin_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-hussin-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Schools">Schools</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="709" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg/220px-Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg/330px-Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg/440px-Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan" title="Great Mosque of Kairouan">Great Mosque of Kairouan</a> (also known as the Mosque of Uqba) in the city of <a href="/wiki/Kairouan" title="Kairouan">Kairouan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, was, particularly from the 9thâ11th century, an important center of Islamic learning with an emphasis on the <a href="/wiki/Maliki" title="Maliki">Maliki</a> Madh'hab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-futureIslam_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-futureIslam-118">[118]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>There are many intellectual traditions within the field of <i><a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Shari'ah</a></i> (Islamic law), often referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Madhhab" title="Madhhab">Madh'habs</a></i> (legal schools). These varied traditions reflect differing viewpoints on some laws and obligations within Islamic law. While one school may see a certain act as a religious obligation, another may see the same act as optional. These schools aren't regarded as sects; rather, they represent differing viewpoints on issues that are not considered the core of Islamic belief. Historians have differed regarding the exact delineation of the schools based on the underlying principles they follow.
</p><p>Many traditional scholars saw Sunni Islam in two groups: <i><a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Ra%27y" title="Ahl al-Ra'y">Ahl al-Ra'y</a></i>, or "people of reason", due to their emphasis on scholarly judgment and discourse; and <i><a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Hadith" title="Ahl al-Hadith">Ahl al-Hadith</a></i>, or "people of traditions", due to their emphasis on restricting juristic thought to only what is found in scripture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Khaldun" title="Ibn Khaldun">Ibn Khaldun</a> defined the Sunni schools as three: the <a href="/wiki/Hanafi" title="Hanafi">Hanafi</a> school representing reason, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E1%BA%92%C4%81hir%C4%AB" title="áºÄhirÄ«">áºÄhirÄ«te</a> school representing tradition, and a broader, middle school encompassing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shafi%27i" title="Shafi'i">Shafi'ite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maliki" title="Maliki">Malikite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hanbali" title="Hanbali">Hanbalite</a> schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate_(Cairo)" title="Mamluk Sultanate (Cairo)">Mamluk Sultanate</a> in Egypt delineated the acceptable Sunni schools as only Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi'i and Hanbali, excluding the áºÄhirÄ« school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> later reaffirmed the official status of four schools as a reaction to the <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shiite</a> character of their ideological and political archrival, the <a href="/wiki/Safavid_dynasty" title="Safavid dynasty">Persian Safavids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chib_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-chib-115">[115]</a></sup> In the contemporary era, former <a href="/wiki/List_of_heads_of_government_of_Sudan" title="List of heads of government of Sudan">Prime Minister of Sudan</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Sadiq_al-Mahdi" title="Al-Sadiq al-Mahdi">Al-Sadiq al-Mahdi</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Amman_Message" title="Amman Message">Amman Message</a> issued by King <a href="/wiki/Abdullah_II_of_Jordan" title="Abdullah II of Jordan">Abdullah II of Jordan</a>, recognize the áºÄhirÄ«s and keep the number of Sunni schools at five.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>The doctrines of the Sunnis are recorded in various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aqida" title="Aqida">creeds</a>, which summarize the most important points in the form of a list in the manner of a <a href="/wiki/Catechism" title="Catechism">Catechism</a>. The individual teaching points differ depending on the author's affiliation to a certain teaching tradition. The most important creeds that explicitly claim to represent the teachings of the Sunnis (<i>ahl as-sunna wal-jama</i> or similar) include:
</p>
<ul><li>The text traced back to <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Hanbal" title="Ahmad ibn Hanbal">Ahmad ibn Hanbal</a>, in which he defined "the characteristics of the believer of the Sunnis" (<i>sifat al-Mu'min min ahl as-Sunna wa-l-jama</i>). The text is handed down in two works in the work <i>á¹¬abaqÄt al-á¸¤anÄbila</i> of the Hanbali Qadi Ibn Abi YaÊ¿la (d. 1131). The first version comes from a treatise on the Sunnah by Ahmad ibn Hanbal's disciple Muhammad ibn Habib al-Andarani, the second is based on Ahmad's disciple Muhammad ibn Yunus al-Sarachhi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></li>
<li>The two creeds of Abu l-Hasan al-AshÊ¿arÄ« in his works <i>MaqÄlÄt al-islÄmÄ«yÄ«n</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> and <i>KitÄb al-IbÄna Ê¿an uá¹£Å«l ad-diyÄna</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kitab_al-Ibana8_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kitab_al-Ibana8-30">[30]</a></sup>  The former is called the teaching of <i>ahl al-á¸¥adÄ«á¹¯ wa-s-sunna</i>, the latter as the teachings of the <i>ahl al-á¸¥aqq wa-s-sunna</i>.</li>
<li>The confession of the Egyptian Hanafi at-TahÄwÄ« (d. 933), also known under the title <i> BayÄn as-sunna wa-l-Ç§amÄÊ¿a </i> ("Presentation of Sunna and Community"). It has received frequent comments from the 13th century onward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></li>
<li>The "Qadiritic Creed" (<i>al-iÊ¿tiqÄd al-QÄdirÄ«</i>) mentioned in the world chronicle <i>al-Muntaáºam </i> by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ibn_al-Jschauz%C4%AB&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ibn al-JschauzÄ« (page does not exist)">Ibn al-JschauzÄ«</a> and referring to the Abbasid caliph <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Q%C4%81dir&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Al-QÄdir (page does not exist)">al-QÄdir</a> (d. 1031) is returned. The caliph <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Qa%27im_(Abbasids)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Al-Qa'im (Abbasids) (page does not exist)">al-QÄ'im</a> is supposed to have read this text, which is shown at the end as the "Doctrine of the Sunnis" (<i>qaul ahl as-sunna wal-jama</i>), in the year 433 <a href="/wiki/Hijri_year" title="Hijri year">Hijra</a> (= 1041/42 AD) which was read in front of a meeting of ascetics and scholars in the caliph's palace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IbnGauzi280198_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-IbnGauzi280198-128">[128]</a></sup></li>
<li>The creed of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Ghaz%C4%81l%C4%AB" title="Al-GhazÄlÄ«">al-GhazÄlÄ«</a> (d. 1111) in his second book of his religious encyclopedia <i>Iá¸¥yÄÊ¾ Ê¿ulÅ«m ad-dÄ«n</i>. It is headed "The Sunni Creed in the Two Phrases of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shah%C4%81da" title="ShahÄda">ShahÄda</a>" (<i>Ê¿AqÄ«dat ahl as-sunna fÄ« kalimatai aÅ¡-Å¡ahÄda</i>) and deals first with the doctrine of God and then the other doctrinal points.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></li>
<li>The confession <i>al-Ê¿AqÄ«da al-WÄsiá¹­Ä«ya</i> by Ibn TaimÄ«ya (1263â1328),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> which later received importance especially among the Wahhabis and the Ahl-i HadÄ«th. It was translated into French by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Laoust" title="Henri Laoust">Henri Laoust</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> by Merlin Swartz into English<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> and by Clemens Wein into German.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Most of the mentioned branches testify to six principal articles of faith known as the six pillars of <i>imÄn</i> (Arabic for "faith"), which are believed to be essential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> These six articles are common that present-day Sunnis agree on, from those who adhere to traditional Sunnism to those who adhere to latter-day movements. Additionally, classical Sunni Islam also outlined numerous other cardinal doctrines since the 8th century, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Aqidah_al-Tahawiyyah" title="Al-Aqidah al-Tahawiyyah">Creed of TahÄwi</a>. Traditionally, these Sunni articles of faith have included the following:
</p>
<ol><li>Belief in the Oneness of God</li>
<li>Belief in the Angels of God</li>
<li>Belief in Holy Books</li>
<li>Belief in the Prophets of God</li>
<li>Belief in Resurrection after Death and the Day of Judgment</li>
<li>Belief in Preordainment (<i>Qadar</i>)</li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="God">God</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God in Islam</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Unity">Unity</span></h4>
<p>At the center of the Sunni creed is <a href="/wiki/Tawhid" title="Tawhid">Tawhid</a>, the belief in the oneness of God. God is a single (<i>fard</i>) God, besides whom there is no other deity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aschari29056_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aschari29056-135">[135]</a></sup> He is single (<i>munfarid</i>), has no partner (<i>Å¡arÄ«k</i>), no opposite (<i>nidd</i>), no counterpart (<i>maá¹¯Ä«l</i>) and no adversary (<i>á¸idd</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> He has neither taken a companion nor children,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aschari29056_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aschari29056-135">[135]</a></sup> neither conceived nor is he conceived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IbnGauzi280198_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-IbnGauzi280198-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>God created everything, the years and times, day and night, light and darkness, the heavens and the earth, all kinds of creatures that are on it, the land and the sea, and everything living, dead and solid. Before he created all of this, he was completely alone, with nothing with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IbnGauzi280198_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-IbnGauzi280198-128">[128]</a></sup> In contrast to his creation, God has a timeless nature. He is beginningless (<i>azalÄ«</i>) because he has existed for all eternity and nothing precedes him, and he is endless (<i>abadÄ«</i>) because he continues to exist without interruption for all eternity. He is the first and the last, as it says in the Quran (Sura 57: 3).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>  God brought forth creation not because he needed it, but to demonstrate his power and as the implement his previous will and his primordial speech.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> God is creator, but has no needs. He does not need food,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> does not feel lonely and does not keep company with anyone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IbnGauzi280198_128-3"><a href="#cite_note-IbnGauzi280198-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transcendence">Transcendence</span></h4>
<p>To absolve God of all anthropomorphism, the Qur'anic statements that "God sat on the throne" (istawÄ Ê¿alÄ l-Ê¿arÅ¡; Surah 7:54; 20: 5) receive a lot of the Sunni creeds attention. The creed of al-QÄdir emphasizes that God did not set himself up on the throne (Ê¿arÅ¡) "in the manner of the rest of the creatures" and that he created this throne, although he did not need it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IbnGauzi280198_128-4"><a href="#cite_note-IbnGauzi280198-128">[128]</a></sup> Al-Ghazali's knowledge of the faith states that the "sitting down" is free from contact (<i>mumÄssa</i>) with the throne. It is not the throne that carries God, but the throne and its bearers are carried through the grace of his power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> According to al-AshÊ¿ari, the Sunnis confess that God is on his throne, but without asking how.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Even if God does not need the throne and what is below, because he spatially occupies everything, including what is above him, the throne and stool (<i> kursÄ« </i>) are a reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Names_and_attributes">Names and attributes</span></h4>
<p>The Sunnis confess that the names of God cannot be said to be anything other than God, as MuÊ¿tazilites and Kharijites claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Rather, they teach that there are correlating attributes (<i> á¹£ifÄt </i>) which exist in each of the names of God mentioned in the Quran: God is alive through life (<i> á¸¥ayÄh </i>), knowing through knowledge (<i>Ê¿ilm</i>), mighty through power (<i>Ê¿qudra</i>), wanting through will (<i>irÄda</i>), hearing through hearing (<i>samÊ¿</i>), seeing through sight (<i>baá¹£ar</i>) and speaking through Speech (<i>kalÄm</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazali10776_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gazali10776-144">[144]</a></sup> The attributes are not identical to God, nor are they anything different from him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Only those attributes are ascribed to God which he ascribed to himself (in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>) or which his prophet ascribed to him. And every attribute that he or his prophet has ascribed to him is a real attribute, not an attribute <a href="/wiki/Majaz" title="Majaz">figuratively</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IbnGauzi280199_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-IbnGauzi280199-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Angels_and_other_spirits">Angels and other spirits</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Siyer-i_Nebi_298a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="777" data-file-width="497" decoding="async" height="344" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Siyer-i_Nebi_298a.jpg/220px-Siyer-i_Nebi_298a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Siyer-i_Nebi_298a.jpg/330px-Siyer-i_Nebi_298a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Siyer-i_Nebi_298a.jpg/440px-Siyer-i_Nebi_298a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Siyer-i_Nebi_298a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammed" title="Muhammed">Muhammed</a> accompanied by the <a href="/wiki/Archangel" title="Archangel">archangels</a> <a href="/wiki/Gabriel" title="Gabriel">Gabriel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_(archangel)" title="Michael (archangel)">Michael</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israfil" title="Israfil">Israfil</a> und <a href="/wiki/Azrael" title="Azrael">Azrael</a>. Turkish <a href="/wiki/Siyer-i_Nebi" title="Siyer-i Nebi">Siyer-i-Nebi</a>-work, 1595</div></div></div>
<p>Sunnis believe in <a href="/wiki/Angels_in_Islam" title="Angels in Islam">angels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aschari29056_135-2"><a href="#cite_note-Aschari29056-135">[135]</a></sup> God hid the angels from the vision of humans, thus they can usually not see them. Just in some special occusations God unveils them for individual humans. Like when the archangel Gabrel appeared to the prophet Muhammad one time in his true form with 600 wings, filling the entire horizon and another time when he was among the circles of the Sahaba, in the form of a white clothed traveller.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>Angels fulfil duties assigned by God. The angel Gabriel has the mission to transmit God's revelations to chosen Prophets. The angel Michael is assigned over rain and plants. The angel <a href="/wiki/Israfil" title="Israfil">Israfil</a> must blow into the trumpet during thunder and the day of resurrection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Furthermore to the angels belong the recording angels, who supervise humans and the angel of death, who takes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruh" title="Ruh">souls</a> (lit. spirits) of the inhabitants of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AqÄ«da_1995_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-AqÄ«da_1995-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike the Mutazilites and the Jahmites,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> the Sunnis believe that <a href="/wiki/Iblis" title="Iblis">Satan</a> whispers doubts to humans and hits them, as the Quran states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aschari29660_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aschari29660-151">[151]</a></sup> But humans, <a href="/wiki/Jinn" title="Jinn">jinn</a>, angels and <a href="/wiki/Shaitan" title="Shaitan">devils</a> are all created by the power of God and bound to his will. Even if humans, jinn, angels and devils aligned to move or stop one atom, they could not succeed without God's will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazali10775_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gazali10775-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Books_of_God">Books of God</span></h3>
<p>The Sunnis further believe in the books of God, sent to the envoys of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahawi2052_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tahawi2052-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aschari29056_135-3"><a href="#cite_note-Aschari29056-135">[135]</a></sup> To them belong the Quran, the Torah, the Gospel and the Psalms<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazali10776_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gazali10776-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Quran is according to Sunni views the speech of God. Who listens to it and regards it as human speech is according to Sunni Creed by at-TahÄwÄ« an <a href="/wiki/Kafir" title="Kafir">infidel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahawi1249_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tahawi1249-154">[154]</a></sup> The Quran as the speech of God is sent down by the âtrustworthy Spiritâ  (<i>ar-rÅ«á¸¥ al-amÄ«n</i>; surah 26:193) and taught by Muhammad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahawi2052_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tahawi2052-153">[153]</a></sup> God sent him down as inspiration (<i>wahy</i>) on his Messenger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahawi1249_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tahawi1249-154">[154]</a></sup> The path of God's speech to the community of Muslims is a multi-stage process: God pronounced it, the angel Gabriel heard it and Mohammed repeated it, Mohammed repeated it to his companions, and the <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Ummah</a> repeated them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IbnGauzi280199_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-IbnGauzi280199-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>As the speech of God, the Quran is according to Sunnism uncreated. The teachings of the creation of the Quran is rejected by Sunnis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahawi2052_153-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tahawi2052-153">[153]</a></sup> Anyone who takes this teaching is regarded as an unbeliever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> The Quran is recited with the tongue, written into books and memorized by the heart, but remains the uncreated speech of God, because it is individable and can not be split by the transmition from heart to paper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazali10776_144-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gazali10776-144">[144]</a></sup>  At-TahÄwÄ« specifies that the Quran is not created like human speech. Rather, it came from God in an unexplained way as a word (<i> qaul </i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahawi1249_154-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tahawi1249-154">[154]</a></sup> Ibn TaimÄ«ya explains that the Quran originated from God and will return (sc. At the end of times) too.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prophets">Prophets</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Messages">Messages</span></h4>
<p>Confessing to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_of_Islam" title="Prophets of Islam">Prophets</a> of God is also part of the Sunni faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aschari29056_135-4"><a href="#cite_note-Aschari29056-135">[135]</a></sup> The first of the prophets is <a href="/wiki/Adam_in_Islam" title="Adam in Islam">Adam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> The original contract (<i> mÄ«á¹¯Äq </i>) that God concluded with him and his descendants according to sura 7:172â173 is a reality according to Sunni belief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> God has taken <a href="/wiki/Abraham_in_Islam" title="Abraham in Islam">Abraham</a> as a friend (<i> á¸«alÄ«l </i>) and talked to <a href="/wiki/Moses_in_Islam" title="Moses in Islam">Moses</a> directly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> The last of the prophets is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohammed" title="Mohammed">Mohammed</a> from the tribe of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quraish" title="Quraish">Quraish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazali10877_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gazali10877-160">[160]</a></sup> The Sunnis do not differentiate between the messengers of God, (By rejecting some of them), but consider everything they have brought to be true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahawi2253_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tahawi2253-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>God called the prophets and presented their truthfulness through obvious miracles. The prophets conveyed God's command and prohibition, his promise and threat, and it is incumbent on people to believe what they have brought to be true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazali10877_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gazali10877-160">[160]</a></sup> God has given people the act of obedience (<i> á¹­ÄÊ¿a </i>) and opposition (<i> maÊ¿á¹£iya </i>) forbidden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> God's right to the acts of obedience is not only an obligation for people through the intellect (<i> bi-muÇ§arrad al-Ê¿aql </i>), but also through it for making it a duty through the oral transmission of his prophets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazali10877_160-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gazali10877-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Muhammad">Muhammad</span></h4>
<p>Muhammad from the tribe of the Quraish is not only the seal of the prophets (<i>á¸«Ätam al-anbiyÄÊ¾</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahawi1249_154-3"><a href="#cite_note-Tahawi1249-154">[154]</a></sup> rather, God placed him above all other prophets and made him Lord of men (<i>saiyid al-baÅ¡ar</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazali10877_160-3"><a href="#cite_note-Gazali10877-160">[160]</a></sup> He is God's chosen servant (<i>Ê¿abd</i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ras%C5%ABl" title="RasÅ«l">Messenger</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">Imam</a> of the godly (<i> imÄm al-atqiyÄÊ¾ </i>) and the beloved of the Lord of the Worlds (<i>á¸¥abÄ«b rabb al-Ê¿ÄlamÄ«n</i>). He is sent with truth (<i>á¸¥aqq</i>), guidance (<i>hudÄ</i>) and light (<i>nÅ«r</i>). God has him with his message to <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a> and Non-Arabs as well as sent to the general public of the <a href="/wiki/Jinn" title="Jinn">jinn</a> and humans and with his <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a>, the earlier religious laws <a href="/wiki/Naskh_(tafsir)" title="Naskh (tafsir)">abrogated</a>, except that which he has confirmed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazali10877_160-4"><a href="#cite_note-Gazali10877-160">[160]</a></sup> Part of the Sunnis path is to follow the traditions (<i>Äá¹¯Är</i>) of Muhammad internally and externally. They prefer his guidance to the guidance of anyone else.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>Muhammad's prophethood is proven by miracles (<i>muÊ¿Ç§izÄt</i>) such as the splitting of the moon. The most obvious miracle is the Quran's <a href="/wiki/I%27jaz" title="I'jaz">inimitability</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> Every claim to prophethood after him is an error or imagination, since Muhammad is the last prophet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahawi1249_154-4"><a href="#cite_note-Tahawi1249-154">[154]</a></sup> Another important point of teaching is the belief in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascension_of_Muhammad" title="Ascension of Muhammad">Muhammad's Ascension</a> (<i>miÊ¿rÄÇ§</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aschari29660_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aschari29660-151">[151]</a></sup>
Accordingly the Prophet went on a nocturnal journey during which his person was transported to heaven while awake and from there to heights, "which God has chosen". God gave him what he had chosen for him and gave him his revelation. God has also blessed him in his life beyond and in this world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahawi1249_154-5"><a href="#cite_note-Tahawi1249-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eschatology">Eschatology</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_grave">In the grave</span></h4>
<p>According to Sunni doctrine, people are questioned in their graves by <a href="/wiki/Munkar_and_Nakir" title="Munkar and Nakir">Munkar and Nakir</a> after death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AqÄ«da_1995_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-AqÄ«da_1995-149">[149]</a></sup> Munkar and NakÄ«r are two terrifying, huge figures who let the person sit upright in his grave with mind and body and then tell him about the oneness of God and the prophethood of Muhammad. They ask him: "Who is your master? What is your religion? Who is your prophet?". They are the two inspectors of the grave and their questioning is the first test (<i> fitna </i>) of humans after death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazali10877_160-5"><a href="#cite_note-Gazali10877-160">[160]</a></sup> The believer will answer to this test: "God is my Lord, Islam is my religion and Mohammed is my prophet." The doubter, on the other hand, will answer: "Oh dear, I don't know. I heard people say something, and that's how I said it.â He is then hit with an iron club so that he utters a loud scream that can be heard by everyone except people and jinn. If people heard him, they would lose consciousness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> Children are also interviewed by Munkar and NakÄ«r as well as people who have disappeared, drowned or were eaten by predatory animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> Deceased Muslims receive the <a href="/wiki/Dua" title="Dua">supplication</a> said for them, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sadaqa" title="Sadaqa">Sadaqa</a> spoken in their name are a favor for them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aschari29660_151-2"><a href="#cite_note-Aschari29660-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sign_of_the_hour">Sign of the hour</span></h4>
<p>Another point of belief are the "signs of the hour" (<i>aÅ¡rÄá¹­ as-sÄÊ¿a</i>) that precede the day of resurrection. This includes the emergence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dajjal" title="Dajjal">Dajjal</a>, the rising of the sun in the west, the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Beast_of_the_Earth" title="Beast of the Earth">Dabba</a> from the earth<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> and the excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Gog_and_Magog" title="Gog and Magog">Gog and Magog</a>. Jesus, the son of Mary, will descend from heaven<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> and kill the Dajjal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Day_of_resurrection">Day of resurrection</span></h4>
<p>On Day of the Resurrection the resurrection (<i> baÊ¿á¹¯ </i>) and the retribution of the deeds take place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> First the bodies of all people, animals and jinn are put back together and revived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> The souls are brought back into the body, the people rise from their graves, barefoot, naked and uncircumcised. The sun is approaching them and they are sweating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>A scales are set up to weigh people's deeds. The scales have two <a href="/wiki/Weighing_scale" title="Weighing scale">scales</a> and one tongue and are as big as several layers of heaven and earth. The weights will have the weight of atoms and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chardal" title="Chardal">mustard seeds</a> in order to realize the accuracy of God's righteousness. The leaves with good deeds (<i>á¸¥asanÄt</i>) are thrown in a beautiful shape into the scales of light and weigh down the scales by the grace (<i>faá¸l</i>) of God, the leaves with bad deeds (<i> saiyiÊ¾Ät </i>) are thrown into the scales of darkness in an ugly form and reduce the weight of the scales through the justice (<i>Ê¿adl</i>) of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_vision_of_God_in_the_hereafter">The vision of God in the hereafter</span></h4>
<p>The teachings of the Sunnis also include the vision of God (<i>ruÊ¾yat AllÄh</i>) in the hereafter, which has similarities with the <i>visio beatifica</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian tradition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> With this teaching the Sunnis set themselves apart from the MuÊ¿tazilites, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zaidiyyah" title="Zaidiyyah">Zaidiyyah</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">philosophers</a> who consider the vision of God intellectually impossible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are differing views among Sunni scholars about the timing and type of the divine vision. Al-Ashari states that God is seen on the day of resurrection, whereby only the believers see him, the unbelievers not because they are kept away from God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AÅ¡Ê¿arÄ«_1931_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-AÅ¡Ê¿arÄ«_1931-176">[176]</a></sup> At-TahÄwÄ«, on the other hand, was of the opinion that the vision of God was a reality for the inmates of Paradise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> Ibn TaimÄ«ya doubles the vision of God: people see God while they are still in the places of the resurrection, and then after entering paradise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ibn_TaimÄ«ya_1999_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ibn_TaimÄ«ya_1999-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>As for the way of seeing God, al-Ash Aari and Ibn Taimiyah emphasized its visual characteristics. Al-Ashari meant that God can be seen with the eyes, just as one sees the moon on the night of the full moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AÅ¡Ê¿arÄ«_1931_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-AÅ¡Ê¿arÄ«_1931-176">[176]</a></sup> Ibn TaimÄ«ya adds that the vision of God is as one sees the sun on a cloudless day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ibn_TaimÄ«ya_1999_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ibn_TaimÄ«ya_1999-178">[178]</a></sup> In the Ê¿AqÄ«da at-TahÄwÄ«s, the transcendence of God is emphasized: the vision can neither be understood nor described, because none of the creatures are like God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> According to al-GhazÄlÄ«'s creed the pious in the hereafter see the essence of God without <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">substance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Accident_(philosophy)" title="Accident (philosophy)">accidents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gazali10776_144-3"><a href="#cite_note-Gazali10776-144">[144]</a></sup> According to the creed of an-NasafÄ«, God is seen neither in one place nor in any direction or distance. There is also no connection to rays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Release_of_the_monotheists_from_hell_and_intercession">Release of the monotheists from hell and intercession</span></h4>
<p>According to the Ibn TaimÄ«ya's creed, the <a href="/wiki/Umma" title="Umma">Umma</a> of Muhammad is the first religious community to enter Paradise,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> Other religious communities also have the opportunity to get to paradise, because God leads whole peoples through the grace of his mercy (<i>aqwÄm</i>) out of hellfire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Hanbal" title="Ahmad ibn Hanbal">Ahmad ibn Hanbal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Ghaz%C4%81l%C4%AB" title="Al-GhazÄlÄ«">al-GhazÄlÄ«</a> declare in their creeds that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monotheist" title="Monotheist">monotheist</a> en (<i>al-muwaá¸¥á¸¥idÅ«n</i>) after being punished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> Al-GhazÄlÄ« adds that through the grace (<i>faá¸l</i>) of God no monotheist remains in hell for all eternity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Engl_1994._p._78_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Engl_1994._p._78-184">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to at-TahÄwÄ«'s creed, this only applies to the serious sinners from Muhammad's ummah: They are in hell, but not forever if they were monotheists at the time of death. What happens to them lies within God: if he wants, he forgives them through his grace (<i>faá¸l</i>), and if he wants, he punishes them in his justice (<i>Ê¿adl</i>) and then brings them through His mercy (<i>raá¸¥ma</i>) and through the intercession of those who obey him out of hell and make them enter the Paradise Garden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup>
</p><p>The intercession (<i>Å¡afÄÊ¿a</i>) of the Messenger of God and its effect on those of his ummah who have committed serious sins is a fixed teaching point of the Sunni faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> The Prophet reserved the intercession especially for them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> According to al-GhazÄlÄ«, the Sunni believer has a total of the intercession of the prophets, then the scholars, then the martyrs, then to believe the other believers in accordance with their dignity and their rank in God. Those of the believers who have no advocate will be brought out of hell by the grace of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Engl_1994._p._78_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-Engl_1994._p._78-184">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_predestination">The predestination</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Extent_of_the_predestination">Extent of the predestination</span></h4>
<p>According to Sunni doctrine, everything that happens happens through God's decision (<i>qadÄÊ¾</i>) and predestination (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qadar" title="Qadar">Qadar</a>) or his determination (<i>taqdÄ«r</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> Predestination includes the predestination of good and bad, sweet and bitter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tahawi2253_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tahawi2253-161">[161]</a></sup> God has that The measured (<i>qadar</i>) of creatures and determined their time of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> He makes his creatures sick and heals them, lets them die and makes them alive, while the creatures themselves have no power over it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IbnGauzi280198_128-5"><a href="#cite_note-IbnGauzi280198-128">[128]</a></sup> God lets die without fear and brings to life without exertion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>  The one who dies dies on the appointed date, even if he is killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aschari29660_151-3"><a href="#cite_note-Aschari29660-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>God has written the things predestined for the creatures on the well-kept tablet (<i>al-lauá¸¥ al-maá¸¥fÅ«áº</i>). The pen she wrote is the first thing God created. God commanded him to write down what will be until the day of resurrection. The pen has already dried out and the scrolls are rolled up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> Everything that was written on it in ancient times is immutable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p>God is righteous in his judgments (<i>aqá¸iya</i>), but his righteousness cannot be decided by analogy with the righteousness of people, because unjust actions for people are only conceivable with regard to someone else's property, but God does not encounter someone else's property anywhere so that he could behave unfairly to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> The principle of predestination is God's mystery with regard to his creatures. No <a href="/wiki/Archangel" title="Archangel">Archangel</a> and no prophet is informed of this. Reflecting on predestination leads to destruction and is a step toward rebellion against God because He has hidden the knowledge about it from people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Blessed_and_the_Damned">The Blessed and the Damned</span></h4>
<p>It is made easy for everyone for what they were created for. Blessed are whose who are saved by God's judgment (<i>qaá¸ÄÊ¾ AllÄh</i>), condemned are whose who are condemned by the judgment of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-195">[195]</a></sup> God created paradise and hell above all else; then he created the people who are worthy of them. He has designated some out of generosity (<i>faá¸lan</i>) for paradise, the others out of justice (<i>Ê¿adlan</i>) for hell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> God has always known the number of those who go to paradise and the number of those who go to hell. This number is neither increased nor decreased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_195-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-195">[195]</a></sup> When God creates the body of the embryo, he sends an angel to him who writes down his livelihood (<i>rizq</i>), the hour he dies, his deeds and whether he is a damned (<i>Å¡aqÄ«</i>) or a blessed (<i>saÊ¿Ä«d</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sunni believer does not doubt his belief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> Humans neither know how they are registered by God (whether as believers or unbelievers), nor how it ends with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IbnGauzi281200_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-IbnGauzi281200-199">[199]</a></sup> God is also the converter of hearts (<i>muqallib al-qulÅ«b</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup>  Therefore it is recommended to say: âA believer, if God is willingâ or âI hope that I am a Believerâ. Such a way of expression does not make people into doubters, because by that, they only mean that their otherworldly fate and their end are hidden from them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IbnGauzi281200_199-1"><a href="#cite_note-IbnGauzi281200-199">[199]</a></sup>  The Sunnis do not speak to any of the people who pray to the Kaaba, to paradise or hell,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> because of a good deed or a sin he has committed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sunni_view_of_hadith">Sunni view of <i>hadith</i></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2840" data-file-width="3301" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg/250px-Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg/375px-Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg/500px-Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cour_mosquee_Suleymaniye_Istanbul.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/S%C3%BCleymaniye_Mosque" title="SÃ¼leymaniye Mosque">SÃ¼leymaniye Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Quran as it exists today in book form was compiled by Muhammad's companions (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahabah" title="Sahabah">Sahabah</a></i>) within a handful of months of his death, and is accepted by all sects of Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> Many matters of belief and daily life were not directly prescribed in the Quran, but were actions observed by Muhammad and the early Muslim community. Later generations sought out <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral traditions</a> regarding the early history of Islam, and the practices of Muhammad and his first followers, and wrote them down so that they might be preserved. These recorded oral traditions are called hadith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> Muslim scholars have through the ages sifted through the hadith and evaluated the chain of narrations of each tradition, scrutinizing the trustworthiness of the narrators and judging the strength of each hadith accordingly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kutub_al-Sittah"><i>Kutub al-Sittah</i></span></h3>
<p><i>Kutub al-Sittah</i> are six books containing collections of hadiths. Sunni Muslims accept the hadith collections of <a href="/wiki/Sahih_al-Bukhari" title="Sahih al-Bukhari">Bukhari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muslim_ibn_al-Hajjaj" title="Muslim ibn al-Hajjaj">Muslim</a> as the most authentic (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahih" title="Sahih">sahih</a></i>), and while accepting all hadiths verified as authentic, grant a slightly lesser status to the collections of other recorders. Four other hadith collections are also held in particular reverence by Sunni Muslims, making a total of six:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sahih_al-Bukhari" title="Sahih al-Bukhari">Sahih al-Bukhari</a> of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Bukhari" title="Muhammad al-Bukhari">Muhammad al-Bukhari</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sahih_Muslim" title="Sahih Muslim">Sahih Muslim</a> of <a href="/wiki/Muslim_ibn_al-Hajjaj" title="Muslim ibn al-Hajjaj">Muslim ibn al-Hajjaj</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunan_al-Sughra" title="Sunan al-Sughra">Sunan al-Sughra</a> of <a href="/wiki/Al-Nasa%27i" title="Al-Nasa'i">Al-Nasa'i</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunan_Abu_Dawud" title="Sunan Abu Dawud">Sunan Abu Dawud</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Dawood" title="Abu Dawood">Abu Dawood</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jami%27_at-Tirmidhi" title="Jami' at-Tirmidhi">Jami' at-Tirmidhi</a> of <a href="/wiki/Al-Tirmidhi" title="Al-Tirmidhi">Al-Tirmidhi</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunan_Ibn_Majah" title="Sunan Ibn Majah">Sunan Ibn Majah</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Majah" title="Ibn Majah">Ibn Majah</a></li></ul>
<p>There are also other collections of hadith which also contain many authentic hadith and are frequently used by scholars and specialists. Examples of these collections include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Musannaf_of_Abd_al-Razzaq" title="Musannaf of Abd al-Razzaq">Musannaf of Abd al-Razzaq</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%27Abd_ar-Razzaq_as-San%27ani" title="'Abd ar-Razzaq as-San'ani">'Abd ar-Razzaq as-San'ani</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Musnad_Ahmad_ibn_Hanbal" title="Musnad Ahmad ibn Hanbal">Musnad</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Hanbal" title="Ahmad ibn Hanbal">Ahmad ibn Hanbal</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mustadrak_al-Hakim" title="Mustadrak al-Hakim">Mustadrak</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hakim_al-Nishaburi" title="Hakim al-Nishaburi">Al Haakim</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Muwatta_Imam_Malik" title="Muwatta Imam Malik">Muwatta</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imam_Malik" title="Imam Malik">Imam Malik</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahih_Ibn_Hibbaan" title="Sahih Ibn Hibbaan">Sahih Ibn Hibbaan</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sahih_Ibn_Khuzaymah" title="Sahih Ibn Khuzaymah">Sahih Ibn Khuzaymah</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Khuzaymah" title="Ibn Khuzaymah">Ibn Khuzaymah</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sunan_al-Darimi" title="Sunan al-Darimi">Sunan al-Darimi</a> of <a href="/wiki/Al-Darimi" title="Al-Darimi">Al-Darimi</a></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sunni_State_institutions">Sunni State institutions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:98px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TRT_Diyanet_kurumsal_logo.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1406" data-file-width="774" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/TRT_Diyanet_kurumsal_logo.png/96px-TRT_Diyanet_kurumsal_logo.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/TRT_Diyanet_kurumsal_logo.png/145px-TRT_Diyanet_kurumsal_logo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/TRT_Diyanet_kurumsal_logo.png/192px-TRT_Diyanet_kurumsal_logo.png 2x" width="96"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TRT_Diyanet_kurumsal_logo.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>TRT Diyanet kurumsal logo</div></div></div>
<p>One of the most important teaching institutions of Sunni Islam worldwide is the <a href="/wiki/Al-Azhar_University" title="Al-Azhar University">Azhar</a> in Egypt. Article 32b, paragraph 7 of the Egyptian Azhar Law of 1961 stipulates that the Azhar "follows the path of the Sunnis" (<i>manhaÇ§ ahl as-sunna wa-l-jamÄÊ¿a</i>), Umma has agreed to the foundations of the religion and applications of fiqh, with its four <a href="/wiki/Madhhab" title="Madhhab">disciplines</a>. Only those who stick to the paths of their science and behavior can become a âMember of the Council of Great Scholarsâ (<i>haiÊ¾at kibÄr al-Ê¿ulamÄÊ¾</i>), among whom the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Imam_of_al-Azhar" title="Grand Imam of al-Azhar">Grand Imam of al-Azhar</a> is elected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> Zitouna University in Tunisia and University of al-Qarawiyyin in Morocco are recognized. They are also mentioned, along with the Azhar, in the final document of the Sunni Conference in Grozny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grozny_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grozny-207">[207]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another body that claims to speak on behalf of Sunnism is the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Senior_Scholars_(Saudi_Arabia)" title="Council of Senior Scholars (Saudi Arabia)">Council of Senior Religious Scholars</a> founded in Saudi Arabia in 1971. In the past, the committee has expressed several times on fatwas about the Sunni-membership of certain Islamic groups within. In 1986 it published a fatwa excluding the <a href="/wiki/Al-Ahbash" title="Al-Ahbash">AhbÄsh</a> community from Sunnism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> The Islamic World League in Mecca, also funded by Saudi Arabia, made a resolution from 1987 that it regards Sunnism as the pure teachings at the time of the Messenger and the rightful existence of the Caliphate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Turkish <a href="/wiki/Directorate_of_Religious_Affairs" title="Directorate of Religious Affairs">Directorate of Religious Affairs</a> (<i>Diyanet Ä°Åleri BaÅkanlÄ±ÄÄ±</i>), follows the religious policy of the Ottoman Empire, providing a Sunni interpretation of Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lord138_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lord138-210">[210]</a></sup> Plans by the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_National_Unity" title="Committee of National Unity">Committee of National Unity</a> in the 1960s to convert the Diyanet authority into a non-denominational institution that also integrated the <a href="/wiki/Alevism" title="Alevism">Alevis</a>, failed because of resistance from conservative Sunni clergy inside and outside the Diyanet authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> Since the 1990s, the Diyanet authority has presented itself as an institution that stands above the denominations (<i>mezhepler Ã¼stÃ¼</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lord138_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lord138-210">[210]</a></sup> The religious education organized by the authority at the Turkish schools is based exclusively on the Sunni understanding of Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-image_of_the_Sunnis">Self-image of the Sunnis</span></h2>
<h3><span id="As_the_.22saved_sect.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='As_the_"saved_sect"'>As the "saved sect"</span></h3>
<p>A well-known <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a>, which is to be interpreted as <a href="/wiki/Vaticinium_ex_eventu" title="Vaticinium ex eventu">Vaticinium ex eventu</a>, says that the Muslim <a href="/wiki/Umma" title="Umma">Umma</a> will split into 73 sects, only one of which will be saved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> The Sunnis have the idea that they are this âsaved sectâ (âfirqa nÄ niyaâ). For example, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Mansur_al-Baghdadi" title="Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi">Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi</a> (d. 1037) explains at the beginning of his <a href="/wiki/Heresiology" title="Heresiology">heresiographical</a> work <i> al-Farq baina l-firaq </i> ("The difference between the sects" ) that there are 20 <a href="/wiki/Rafida" title="Rafida">Rafiditic</a>, 20 <a href="/wiki/Kharijites" title="Kharijites">Kharijite</a>, 20 <a href="/wiki/Qadariyah" title="Qadariyah">Qadaritic</a>, 3 <a href="/wiki/Murji%27ah" title="Murji'ah">Murjiite</a>, 3 NadjÄritic, 3 <a href="/wiki/Karramiyya" title="Karramiyya">karramitic</a> and furthermore Bakriyya, Dirariyyya and <a href="/wiki/Jahm_bin_Safwan" title="Jahm bin Safwan">JahmÄ«ya</a>. These are the 72 erring sects. The 73rd sect that is the âsaved sectâ are the Sunnis (<i>ahl as-sunna wa-l-jamaÊ¿a</i>). According to al-Baghdadi, they are composed of two groups, namely the followers of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ra%27y" title="Ra'y">Ra'y</a> and the followers of the hadith. They agreed on the fundamentals of religion (<i>uá¹£Å«l ad-dÄ«n</i>). There were only differences in the derivations (<i>furÅ«Ê¿</i>) from the norms regarding the question of what <a href="/wiki/Halal" title="Halal">permitted</a> and what <a href="/wiki/Haram" title="Haram">forbidden</a> is. These differences are not so great that they considered each other to have strayed from the right path.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_center_of_Muslims">As center of Muslims</span></h3>
<p>Later Sunni scholars also present the Sunnis as the center of Muslim community. The idea already appears to some extent in the AshÊ¿arite Ê¿Abd al-QÄhir al-BaghdÄdÄ«, who emphasizes on several dogmatic questions that the Sunnis hold a position that lies in the middle between the positions of the other Islamic groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> An example is the question of predestination (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qadar" title="Qadar">Qadar</a>), in which, according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kasb" title="Kasb">Kasb</a> theory, you hold exactly the middle between the two extreme positions of the Jabriyya and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qadariyya" title="Qadariyya">Qadariyya</a>.
</p><p>The Hanbali scholar <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Taymiyyah" title="Ibn Taymiyyah">Ibn Taymiyyah</a> (d. 1328), who was otherwise known for his uncompromising attitude, also adhered to this view. He said that the Sunnis represented "the middle among the sects of the <a href="/wiki/Umma" title="Umma">Umma</a>" (<i> al-wasaá¹­ fÄ« firaq al-umma </i>), just as the Islamic Umma is the middle between the other religious communities. He illustrates this with the following examples:
</p>
<ul><li>When it comes to the attributes of God, the Sunnis stand in the middle between the Jahmiyya, who completely drains God of attributes, and the Muschabbiha, who make God similar to creation,</li>
<li>in the works of God they stand in the middle between the Qadariyya and the Jabriyya,</li>
<li>on the question of the threat from God (<i>waÊ¿id Allah</i>) they stand in the middle between the Murdschi'a and the WaÊ¿Ä«diyya, a subgroup of the Qadariyya,</li>
<li>When it comes to the question of faith and religion, they stand in the middle between Haruiyya (= Kharijites) and <a href="/wiki/Mu%CA%BFtazila" title="MuÊ¿tazila">MuÊ¿tazila</a> on the one hand and Murji'a and Jahmiyya on the other,</li>
<li>and with regard to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahaba" title="Sahaba">Companions of the Prophets</a> they are in the middle between <a href="/wiki/Rafida" title="Rafida">Rafidites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kharijites" title="Kharijites">Kharijites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The Hanafi scholar <a href="/wiki/Ali_al-Qari" title="Ali al-Qari">Ê¿AlÄ« al-QÄrÄ«</a> (d. 1606) continued this idea later. In his anti-Shiite pamphlet <i>Å amm al-alawÄriá¸ fÄ« á¸amm ar-rawÄfiá¸</i> he quotes a tradition according to which <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ê¿AlÄ« ibn AbÄ« TÄlib</a> said: "Two kinds of people perish on me: the exaggerated lover and the exaggerated hater." He notes that the exaggerated lover is the Rafidites and the exaggerated hater is the Kharijit. The Sunni, on the other hand, loves Ê¿AlÄ« in high esteem and is thus in the balanced middle (<i> al-wasaá¹­ allaá¸Ä« huwa al-qisá¹­ </i>). This relates al-Qari to the Qur'anic sura 2: 143, in which it is said that God made the Muslims a community standing in the middle (<i> umma wasaá¹­ </i>). Since the Sunnis stay away from the exaggeration described in the traditional Ê¿AlÄ« saying, al-QÄrÄ« believes that they are also the actual "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shia" title="Shia">Party of Ê¿AlÄ«s</a>" (<i> Å¡Ä«Ê¿at Ê¿AlÄ« </i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_the_essential_bearers_of_Islamic_science_and_culture">As the essential bearers of Islamic science and culture</span></h3>
<p>Ê¿Abd al-QÄhir al-BaghdÄdÄ« portrays the Sunnis in his work <i>al-Farq baina l-firaq</i> as the actual bearers of Islamic science and culture. Of all the sciences, knowledge and efforts of which Muslims are proud, al-BaghdÄdÄ« explains that the Sunnis have amajor share.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> In the last chapter of his book, al-Baghdadi also relates this to building activity in Islamic countries. He believes that the Sunnis with their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosques" title="Mosques">mosques</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madrasa" title="Madrasa">madrasas</a>, palaces, factories and hospitals have achieved an unattainable position because none of the non-Sunnis have performed such services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Rivalry_between_Ash.CA.BFar.C4.ABya_and_Salaf.C4.ABya_and_the_2016_Sunni_conferences"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rivalry_between_AshÊ¿arÄ«ya_and_SalafÄ«ya_and_the_2016_Sunni_conferences">Rivalry between AshÊ¿arÄ«ya and SalafÄ«ya and the 2016 Sunni conferences</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ahmed_el-Tayeb_May_2015_(17963337671).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="738" data-file-width="639" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Ahmed_el-Tayeb_May_2015_%2817963337671%29.jpg/220px-Ahmed_el-Tayeb_May_2015_%2817963337671%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Ahmed_el-Tayeb_May_2015_%2817963337671%29.jpg/330px-Ahmed_el-Tayeb_May_2015_%2817963337671%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Ahmed_el-Tayeb_May_2015_%2817963337671%29.jpg/440px-Ahmed_el-Tayeb_May_2015_%2817963337671%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ahmed_el-Tayeb_May_2015_(17963337671).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ahmed_el-Tayeb" title="Ahmed el-Tayeb">Ahmed el-Tayeb</a>, Great-Imam of Azhar, was one of the most important participants of the Sunni-conference in Grozny, distanced himself from the declaration</div></div></div>
<p>Since the second half of the 20th century, there have been fierce clashes within the Sunni camps between <i><a href="/wiki/Ash%27ari" title="Ash'ari">AshÊ¿arites</a></i> on the one hand and <i><a href="/wiki/Salafi_movement" title="Salafi movement">Salafiyya</a></i> on the other, who exclude each other from Sunnism. In <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, the AshÊ¿arite scholar Sirajuddin Abbas (d. 1980) wrote several books in the 1960s in which he explicitly excluded the <i>Ahl as-salaf</i> from Sunnism. Among other things, he argued that there was no Salafi madhhab in the first 300 years of Islam. From this he deduced that those who called other Muslims to obey the Salafi <i>madhhab</i>, were promoting a <i>madhhab</i> which did not even exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> In his view, only the AshÊ¿arites were real Sunnis. Abbas' books served as the theological basis for anti-Salafist campaigns in <a href="/wiki/Aceh" title="Aceh">Aceh</a> in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> During these campaigns, various Salafist schools in Aceh were closed by the provincial government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup>
</p><p>Apparently because of doubts about the <i>SalafÄ«ya</i> affiliation with Sunniism, the "Standing Committee on Scientific Investigation and Fatwa Issuing" in <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> issued a fatwa stating that they consider the Salafis as Sunnis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> Like many AshÊ¿arites, the Salafis believe their teachings are the only true form of Sunnism, and hence reject the Asharites and <a href="/wiki/Maturidi" title="Maturidi">Maturidites</a> as part of Sunnism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> An example is the Saudi scholar <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_al-Uthaymeen" title="Muhammad ibn al-Uthaymeen">Muhammad Ibn al-Ê¿UthaimÄ«n</a>, who in his 2001 published commentary on <i>AqÄ«da WÄsiá¹­Ä«ya</i> by <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Taymiyyah" title="Ibn Taymiyyah">Ibn Taimiyya</a> expressed the opinion that Ash'arites and MÄturÄ«dites would not count among the Sunnis, because their doctrine of attributes would be in contrast to the doctrine of the Prophet and his companions. For this reason, the view that three groups belong to Sunnism should also be rejected. Sunnis are only those who are <i>salaf</i> in terms of belief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup>
</p><p>The accusation by some <a href="/wiki/Wahhabism" title="Wahhabism">Wahhabis</a> that the AshÊ¿arites were not Sunnis was subject of a <a href="/wiki/Fatwa" title="Fatwa">fatwa</a> by the "Egyptian Fatwa Office" in July 2013. In its fatwa, the office rejected this accusation, affirming that the AshÊ¿arites still represented the "multitude of <a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">scholars</a>" (<i>jumhÅ«r al-Ê¿ulamÄÊ¾</i>), and stressed out that they were the ones who in the past rejected the arguments of the <a href="/wiki/Mulhid" title="Mulhid">atheists</a> (<i>Å¡ubuhÄt al-malÄá¸¥ida</i>). Anyone who declares them to be unbelieving or who doubts their orthodoxy should fear for their religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> On the same day, the fatwa office made clear in a fatwa that, according to their understanding, the <i>Ahl as-Sunna wa-l-jama</i> only refer to those Muslims who are AshÊ¿arites or Maturidites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daralifta2366_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-Daralifta2366-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>The rivalry between <i>AshÊ¿arÄ«yya</i> and <i>Salafiyya</i> became visible again at the two Sunni conferences in 2016, which were directed against the terror of the IS organization. The <a href="/wiki/2016_international_conference_on_Sunni_Islam_in_Grozny" title="2016 international conference on Sunni Islam in Grozny">first conference</a> with the title "Who are the Ahl al-Sunna wa al-jama?" took place in the Chechen capital <a href="/wiki/Grozny" title="Grozny">Grozny</a> in August 2016 under the patronage of <a href="/wiki/Ramzan_Kadyrov" title="Ramzan Kadyrov">Ramzan Kadyrov</a>. Numerous religious figures from <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Federation" title="Russian Federation">Russian Federation</a> took part, including <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_el-Tayeb" title="Ahmed el-Tayeb">Ahmed el-Tayeb</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Imam_of_al-Azhar" title="Grand Imam of al-Azhar">Grand Imam</a> the Azhar, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sheikh_Aboobacker_Ahmed" title="Sheikh Aboobacker Ahmed">Sheikh Aboobacker Ahmed</a>, the Grand Mufti of India who is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Barelvi" title="Barelvi">Barelvi</a> movement. According to its organizers, the conference should "mark a blessed turning point in efforts to correct the serious and dangerous distortion of religion by those extremists trying to usurp the venerable name of <i>Ahl al-Sunna wa-al-Jama'a</i>, him to coin exclusively on itself and to exclude its true representatives from it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grozny_207-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grozny-207">[207]</a></sup> In the final declaration the Salafis and <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamist</a> groups like <a href="/wiki/Muslim_Brotherhood" title="Muslim Brotherhood">Muslim Brotherhood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hizb_ut-Tahrir" title="Hizb ut-Tahrir">Hizb ut-Tahrir</a>, etc. and the <i><a href="/wiki/Takfiri" title="Takfiri">Takfiri</a></i> organisations like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">ISIL</a> were excluded from Sunni Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-islam.in.ua_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-islam.in.ua-227">[227]</a></sup> In response to this, various prominent <i>Salafiyya</i> figures held a counter-conference in <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a> in November 2016 under the title "The Correct Meaning of Sunnism" (<i>al-MafhÅ«m aá¹£-á¹£aá¸¥Ä«á¸¥ li-ahl as-sunna wa-l-jama</i>), in which they also distanced themselves from extremist groups, but at the same time insisted that <i>Salafiyya</i> was not only part of Sunnism, but represented Sunnism itself. The conference was chaired by Ahmad ibn Murabit, Grand Mufti of <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> A few days later, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Imam_of_al-Azhar" title="Grand Imam of al-Azhar">Grand Imam of Al-Azhar</a> <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_el-Tayeb" title="Ahmed el-Tayeb">Ahmed el-Tayeb</a> publicly distanced himself from the final declaration of the Grozny conference, reiterating that he had not participated in it and stressed that he naturally viewed the Salafists as Sunnis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup>
</p>
